Maturidi's Refutation of Manichaean Dualism
Maturidi refutes the Manichaean position by internal logical contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of the Manichaean doctrine, focusing on the contradiction of finite parts making an infinite whole.

Core Idea
Self-contradiction is the death of dualism.

Having presented the Manichaean worldview, Maturidi now demonstrates its internal inconsistency. Without appealing to divine revelation, he shows that the doctrine logically self-destructs. The key move is exposing the confusion between infinite and finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read from where Maturidi begins his critique: 'قال الشيخ رحمه الله: ومن تأمل القول...' to the end.
Notice his opening statement: the doctrine is contradictory without need for external evidence.
2
Identify the main contradiction
Maturidi points out that the Manichaeans simultaneously affirm finitude and infinity for the same entities. Find this in the text.
The key phrase: 'أراك النهاية من الوجوه وأثبتها من وجه'.
3
Understand the argument about finite parts
Maturidi argues that if light is infinite but has finite parts (e.g., finite at the point of meeting), then the whole cannot be infinite. Consider the logic.
He says: 'جعل المتناهى غير المتناهي' and argues that finite parts cannot sum to an infinite whole.
4
Reflect on the implications
This refutation shows that dualism is not a viable alternative to monotheism. It collapses under its own premises.
Maturidi's method is to let the opponent's own statements produce the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في صفة أقاويل الثنوية أولاً، أقاويل المنانية وبيان فسادها. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: زعمت المنانية أن الأشياء على ما عليه من امتزاج النور والظلمة، وكانا متباينين: النور في العلو لا يتناهى في أربع جهات شمال وجنوب وصبا ودبور، والظلمة في السفل كذلك، ولها من جهة الالتقاء تناه، فقبعت الظلمة على النور فامتزجا، فكان العالم من امتزاجها على قدر الامتزاج، ولكل واحد منها خمسة أجناس: حمرة وبياض وصفرة وسواد وخضرة، فكل شيء مما جاء من هذا الجنس من جوهر النور فهو خير، وما كان من جوهر الظلمة فهو شر. وكذلك لكل واحد منها حواس خمس: سمع وبصر وذائق وحاسة الشم واللمس، فما أدرك جوهر النور بها فهو خير، وما أدرك جوهر الظلمة فهو شر. وللنور روح، وللظلمة روح، وروح الظلمة يسمى همامة، وهي حية، فغلب العالم ليحبس النور فيها، والنور ليس بحساس، وما كان منه يكون بالطبع ويكون خيرا كله، والهامة حساسة، وسيصير كل واحد منها إلى حيزه. ثم وجد الأشياء أصفاها، وأسفلها أكدرها، ومن طبعها الخفة والثقل، وأمرهما على التنافر، إذ الخفيف يعلو صعدا والثقيل ينحدر سفلا، فيمر الدهر، إذ كانا كذلك يتخلصان من وجه التناهى كما امتزجا. قال الشيخ رحمه الله: ومن تأمل القول وجده كله متناقضا، من غير أن يحتاج إلى تكلف الدلالة على إبطال القول سوى تفسيره. أول شيء به أنه أراك النهاية من الوجوه وأثبتها من وجه، فجعل المتناهى غير المتناهي، إذ النهاية حد، والحد قصر عما هو أعظم منه، وذلك تدبير غيره فيه، وهو دليل حدث جانب منه، وذلك جزء، وبعيد كون كلية الأجزاء المتناهية غير متناهية، لأن ذلك المعنى يتمكن في كل جزء منها يتصل، على أن كل واحد منهما في الوجوه التي لا تتناهى إما أن يكون الآخر فيها فيبطل قوله: امتزجا من جانب، بل كانا غير متناه.
A question concerning the description of the sayings of the Dualists: first, the sayings of the Manichaeans and the exposition of their corruption. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: The Manichaeans claimed that things are as they are from the mixture of light and darkness, and they were distinct: light in the high, infinite in four directions: north, south, east, and west; and darkness in the low similarly, and it has from the direction of meeting a finitude. Then darkness crouched upon light, and they mixed, so the world came from their mixture according to the amount of mixture. And each of them has five kinds: redness, whiteness, yellowness, blackness, and greenness. So everything that comes from this kind from the substance of light is good, and what is from the substance of darkness is evil. Likewise, each of them has five senses: hearing, sight, taste, the sense of smell, and touch. So what it perceives of the substance of light through them is good, and what it perceives of the substance of darkness is evil. And light has a spirit, and darkness has a spirit, and the spirit of darkness is called 'Hammāma', and it is a snake. So the world overcame to imprison light in it, and light is not sensitive, and what is from it is by nature and is all good, and the snake (Hammāma) is sensitive, and each of them will return to its own place. Then he found the things: their highest are purest, and their lowest are murkiest, and from their nature are lightness and heaviness, and their affair is based on repulsion, since the light ascends upward and the heavy descends downward. So time passes, while they are thus being purified from the direction of finitude just as they mixed. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: And whoever contemplates the saying finds it all contradictory, without needing to exert oneself in providing evidence for nullifying the saying beyond its interpretation. The first thing in it is that it showed you finitude from some aspects and affirmed it from one aspect, so it made the finite non-finite. For finitude is a limit, and a limit is a restriction from what is greater than it, and that is a management of another in it, and it indicates the occurrence of a part of it, and that is a part. And it is far that the totality of finite parts be non-finite, because that meaning is possible in each part of it that is connected. Moreover, each of the two, in the aspects that are infinite, either the other is in them, which nullifies his saying that they mixed from one side; rather, they were non-finite.
Source pages p. 756

Commentary
This passage is an exposition and refutation of Manichaean dualism. First, Maturidi presents their doctrine: the universe results from the mixing of light (infinite, above) and darkness (finite, below). Darkness invaded light, and the mixture produced the world. Both light and darkness have five genera (colors) and five senses. Light is good, darkness is evil. Light has a spirit; darkness has a spirit called 'Hammāma' (a serpent) that seeks to imprison light. Eventually, each element will separate and return to its own realm. Then Maturidi launches his refutation: the entire doctrine is self-contradictory without needing external evidence. The first contradiction is that the Manichaeans simultaneously affirm finitude and infinity for the same entities. They claim light is infinite in four directions but darkness has finitude at the point of meeting. But if both are infinite, they cannot mix; if one is finite, it implies a limitation imposed by another, indicating createdness. Furthermore, if the parts are finite, the whole cannot be infinite. This reasoning shows the incoherence of the dualistic cosmogony.

Key Terms
متناه
غير متناه
حد
جزء
كلية
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dualism is a coherent alternative to monotheism because good and evil coexist.
Correction: Maturidi shows dualism is logically self-defeating: it posits both infinite and finite attributes for the same principles, which leads to contradictions.

Checkpoint
What is the first contradiction Maturidi identifies in the Manichaean doctrine?
A) They claim light and darkness are both good.
B) They assert that light is infinite in some directions but finite in others, and simultaneously that darkness is finite, leading to a paradox of finite parts making an infinite whole.
C) They believe the world was created from nothing.
D) They deny the existence of the five senses.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by exposing that its own claims about infinitude and finitude are logically incompatible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi shows that a false belief collapses under its own contradictions. What belief do you hold about yourself—your identity, your worth, or your purpose—that might contain a hidden contradiction? Are you brave enough to examine it with this same logical honesty?
?
The Manichaeans assumed two ultimate principles. But Maturidi forces us to see that infinite beings cannot interact finitely without absurdity. How does this reshape your understanding of Allah's absolute oneness? What in your life still acts as if there is a second power competing with Allah?
?
If merely explaining a falsehood reveals its absurdity, what does that say about the clarity of truth? When was the last time you honestly articulated your own beliefs to yourself? Would they survive the same scrutiny Maturidi applies here? Commit today to writing down one core belief and checking it for internal consistency.

The Manichaean Doctrine of Light and Darkness
Maturidi reports the Manichaean dualist cosmology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Manichaean dualist cosmology as reported by Imam Maturidi, including its account of the origin of the world, the nature of good and evil, and the eventual separation of light and darkness.

Core Idea
Manichaeism explains all reality as a mixture of eternal light and darkness.

Imam Maturidi presents the Manichaean worldview not as a straw man but as a coherent system that must be understood before being refuted. This passage outlines their dualist cosmology, where two eternal principles of light and darkness mix to form the physical world, and moral values are determined by which substance a thing originates from. Let us examine their claims carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice how Maturidi begins by stating the Manichaean claim about the mixture of light and darkness.
The passage is a direct report, not yet a refutation.
2
Identify the spatial and qualitative differences
The text says light is above, infinite in four directions, while darkness is below and finite at the meeting point. Understand this asymmetry.
This spatial arrangement is key to their cosmology.
3
Note the five kinds and five senses
Each principle has five material kinds (colors) and five senses. The moral value of perceived objects depends on which substance they belong to.
This is how they categorize everything as good or evil.
4
Understand the role of the hamama
Darkness has a spirit called hamama, which is alive and sentient, and it overwhelmed the world to imprison light. Light is passive and all good.
This explains the origin of the mixture.
5
Trace the eventual separation
The passage ends with the natural tendency of light to rise and darkness to sink, leading to gradual separation over time.
This is their eschatology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعمت المنانية أن الأشياء على ما عليه من امتزاج النور والظلمة ، وكانا متباينين : النور في العلو لا يتناهى في أربع جهات شمال وجنوب وصبا ودبور ، والظلمة في السفل كذلك، ولها من جهة الالتقاء تناه ، قبعت الظلمة على النور فامتزجا ، فكان العالم من امتزاجها على قدر الامتزاج ، ولكل واحد منها خمسة أجناس : حمرة وبياض وصفرة وسواد وخضرة ، فكل شيء مما جاء من هذا الجنس من جوهر النور فهو خير ، وما كان من جوهر الظلمة فهو شر . وكذلك لكل واحد منها حواس خمس : سمع وبصر وذائق وحاسة الشم واللمس ، فما أدرك جوهر النور بها فهو خير ، وما أدرك جوهر الظلمة فهو شر . وللظلمة روح ، وروح همامة ، وهي حية ، فغلب العالم ليحبس النور فيها ، والنور ليس بحساس ، وما كان منه يكون بالطبع ويكون خيرا كله ، والهامة حساسة ، وسيصير كل واحد منها إلى حيزه . ثم وجد الأشياء أصفاها ، وأسفلها أكدرها ، ومن طبعها الخفة والثقل ، وأمرهما على التنافر ، إذ الخفيف يعلو صعدا والثقيل ينحدر سفلا ، فيمر الدهر ، إذ كانا كذلك يتخلصان من وجه التناهى كما امتزجا .
The Manichaeans claimed that things are as they are from the mixture of light and darkness, and they were two distinct things: light in the high, infinite in four directions (north, south, east, west), and darkness in the low likewise, but it has finitude from the side of meeting. Darkness covered light and they mixed, so the world came to be from their mixture according to the amount of mixture. And for each of them there are five kinds: redness, whiteness, yellowness, blackness, and greenness. So everything that comes from this kind from the substance of light is good, and what is from the substance of darkness is evil. Likewise, for each of them there are five senses: hearing, sight, taste, smell, and touch. So what perceives the substance of light through them is good, and what perceives the substance of darkness is evil. And darkness has a spirit, and the spirit of the hamama, and it is alive, so it overwhelmed the world to imprison light in it, and light is not sentient, and what is from it is by nature and is all good, while the hamama is sentient, and each will return to its own place. Then the things were found: their highest is purest, and their lowest is murkiest, and by their nature lightness and heaviness, and their affair is upon mutual repulsion, since the light rises upward and the heavy descends downward, and time passes while they are thus, being delivered from the side of finitude just as they mixed.
Source pages p. 756

Commentary
This passage presents the Manichaean doctrine as Maturidi reports it. It is an exposition of the dualist worldview that the author will later refute. The Manichaeans assert that reality consists of two eternal, originally separate principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Light is infinite in four directions and located above; darkness is below and finite at the point of contact. Darkness invaded light, resulting in a mixture that is the present world. Each principle has five material kinds (colors) and five senses, which determine the moral value of perceived objects. Darkness has a 'spirit' called the hamama, which is active and sentient, while light is passive and purely good by nature. The hamama imprisoned light in the material world. The mixture will eventually separate, and each principle will return to its proper realm. The passage ends with a description of the natural tendency of light to rise and darkness to sink, leading to gradual separation over time.

Key Terms
نور
ظلمة
امتزاج
جوهر
خير
شر
حاسة
روح
هامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Manichaean doctrine as presented, what is the origin of the world?
A) Creation by a single God
B) Mixture of light and darkness after darkness covered light
C) Emergence from a primordial chaos
D) Emanation from a single source

Key Takeaway
The Manichaean doctrine posits that the world originates from the mixture of two eternal, opposite principles of light and darkness, with moral value determined by substance, and eventual separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the evil in the world, do you ever find yourself treating it as an independent force beyond Allah's control, like the Manichaeans believed? Identify one situation this week where you slipped into that mindset and commit to repeating 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' instead.
?
The Manichaeans believed that light is passive and darkness is active. Have you ever felt spiritually passive, waiting for good to happen, while evil seems actively attacking? What step will you take today to actively pursue good, trusting that Allah is the only active power?

The Contradiction of Finitude and Infinity
Maturidi points out an internal contradiction in the Manichaean claim about finitude and infinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical contradiction Maturidi identifies in the Manichaean claim that two principles are both finite and infinite.

Core Idea
If every part is finite, the whole cannot be infinite.

Maturidi begins his refutation by showing that the Manichaean description of light and darkness as both finite and infinite is self-contradictory. He does not need external proofs—the internal logic collapses on its own. This passage walks through the contradiction step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the contradictory claims
Maturidi states that the Manichaeans affirm finitude from one aspect but also claim infinity. Pinpoint where this happens in the text.
Look for 'أراك النهاية' and 'جعل المتناهى غير المتناهي'.
3
Follow the part-whole argument
Maturidi argues that finitude implies a limit (had), and that limit indicates a part (juz'). He then says it is impossible for the totality of finite parts to be infinite. Trace this reasoning.
Key phrase: 'بعيد كون كلية الأجزاء المتناهية غير متناهية'.
4
Analyze the four-aspect argument
The second part examines infinite aspects of each principle. Maturidi points out that either they occupy each other's infinite directions (contradicting separation) or they do not (making them finite). Understand the logical dilemma.
Focus on 'على أن كل واحد منهما في الوجوه التي لا تتناهى'.
5
Summarize the contradiction
Combine both parts: the Manichaean claim is internally inconsistent because finite parts cannot compose an infinite whole, and the description of infinite aspects leads to a either-or contradiction.
This is the core of Maturidi's internal refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ومن تأمل القول وجده كله متناقضا ، من غير أن يحتاج إلى تكلف الدلالة على إبطال القول سوى تفسيره . أول شيء به أنه أراك النهاية من الوجوه وأثبتها من وجه ، فجعل المتناهى غير المتناهي ، إذ النهاية حد ، والحد قصر عما هو أعظم منه ، وذلك تدبير غيره فيه ، وهو دليل حدث جانب منه ، وذلك جزء ، وبعيد كون كلية الأجزاء المتناهية غير متناهية ، لأن ذلك المعنى يتمكن في كل جزء منها يتصل ، على أن كل واحد منهما في الوجوه التي لا تتناهى إما أن يكون الآخر فيها فيبطل قوله : امتزجا من جانب ، بل كانا إلا من جانب ثم امتزجا ، وإن لم يكن زال كل واحد منهما عن الأوجه الأربعة للآخر ، فصار من تلك الوجوه متناهيا ، والله الموفق .
The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: Whoever ponders the statement finds it all contradictory, without needing to exert effort in demonstrating the invalidity of the statement beyond its interpretation. The first thing in it is that it shows you finitude from some aspects and affirms it from one aspect, so it makes the finite infinite, since finitude is a limit, and a limit is a restriction from what is greater than it, and that is a management by another in it, and it is evidence of the occurrence of a part of it, and that is a part, and it is far that the totality of finite parts should be infinite, because that meaning obtains in every part of it that connects. Moreover, each one of them in the aspects that are infinite: either the other is in them, so it invalidates his statement that they mixed from one side, rather they were only from one side then mixed, and if each of them did not depart from the four aspects of the other, then from those aspects it became finite. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 756

Commentary
This passage is the first point of refutation by Maturidi. He states that the Manichaean doctrine is self-contradictory without need for external arguments. The specific contradiction is regarding finitude and infinity: the Manichaeans claim that light is infinite in four directions but finite at the point of contact with darkness, and similarly darkness is infinite in its own directions but finite at the meeting. Maturidi argues that if something is finite from one aspect, that finitude implies a limit, and that limit is imposed by something else, indicating that the limited part is a part (juz') of a whole. It is impossible for the totality of finite parts to be infinite, because each part shares the property of finitude. Furthermore, if each principle is infinite in some directions, then either each occupies the other's infinite directions (which would preclude separation) or they do not (making them finite in those directions). The argument undermines the Manichaean claim that the two principles are both infinite and finite simultaneously.

Key Terms
تناقض
نهاية
حد
جزء
كلية الأجزاء
متناه
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation is based on external evidence such as scripture or empirical observation.
Correction: Maturidi's refutation is purely internal: he shows that the Manichaean's own statements are self-contradictory without invoking any external proof.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the Manichaean claim about finitude is contradictory?
A) Because light and darkness cannot mix
B) Because if both are infinite in some directions, they must overlap fully, contradicting the claim of mixing from one side only
C) Because finite parts cannot make an infinite whole, and their description implies finite parts
D) Because finitude is a property only of created things

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Manichaean claim of two principles being both finite and infinite is self-contradictory because finite parts cannot sum to an infinite whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify a belief you hold about the world—perhaps about Allah’s attributes or about good and evil—that, if examined closely, contains a logical contradiction similar to the Manichaean claim? Will you apply Maturidi’s method of internal criticism to expose it, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
Maturidi says no external proof is needed, only interpretation of the opponent’s own words. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on external proofs rather than critically examining your own assumptions? What would it look like to turn that scrutiny inward—and what might you discover?

Contradictions in the Natures of Light and Darkness
Maturidi shows contradictions in the alleged natures of light and darkness regarding motion and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the internal contradictions in the Manichaean account of the natures of light and darkness to refute dualism.

Core Idea
Dualism crumbles because darkness rises and light fails to resist—each contradicts its own alleged nature.

In this passage, Maturidi continues his refutation of Manichaean dualism by pointing out two crucial contradictions. The opponents claim that darkness naturally sinks and light naturally rises, yet darkness attacks light by rising. Moreover, light, which should repel darkness, does not resist imprisonment. Maturidi forces the opponent to face the logical consequences of their own assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the rhetorical questions—Maturidi uses them to force reflection.
2
Identify the first contradiction: darkness rising
Maturidi argues that if darkness's nature is to sink, how can it rise to attack light? This shows that darkness has both sinking and rising properties, destroying the foundation of dualism.
The phrase 'kayfa ṣāra al-siflā yadhhabu ṣaʿadan' is key.
3
Identify the second contradiction: light's passivity
If light's nature is to rise away from darkness, why did it not resist being imprisoned? Light is portrayed as weak, contradicting its nature as good and powerful.
Notice the imagery of the hamama binding light.
4
Understand the rhetorical conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the only escape for the dualist is to claim the hamama releases light, which would make darkness the source of good—a further inconsistency.
This highlights the absurdity of the Manichaean position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كان من طبع السفلى التسفل والعلوى العلو ، وذلك معنى التنافر فكيف صار السفلى يذهب صعدا ، وذلك طبع العالى الصافي ، وإليه مرجع وهو معنى العاقبة ، فقد صار من السفلى الأمران جميعا ، فبطل المعنى الذي له لزم القول باثنين . ثم من العلوى النفار إلى العلوى ، ولم يقم بوفاء ذلك ولا امتنع به عما كان بجوهر يتحدر حتى ارتفع عليه ، وخلق العالم بحبسه ، فكيف يطمعون أن يتخلص من يدى الحمامة ، وهى مع ذلك حساسة فعالة بالخيل أوثقته وقيدته وحبسته ، وليست له قوة يتخلص بها ، وبطبعه لم يمتنع عند التخلية ، فكيف يتخلص بعد الوثاق إلا أن يقول : تخلى الهمامة سبيله، فيجعلها فاعلة الخير
Then if it is of the nature of the lower to sink and the higher to rise, and that is the meaning of repulsion, then how did the lower go upward, while that is the nature of the high pure? And to it is the return and it is the meaning of the consequence? So from the lower there have come both matters, thus the meaning for which the statement of duality was necessary is invalidated. Then from the high there is aversion to the high, and it did not fulfill that nor did it refrain from what was by substance descending until it rose above it, and he created the world by imprisoning it, so how do they hope that it will escape from the hands of the hamama, while it is sentient, acting forcefully? It bound him, tied him, and imprisoned him, and he has no power to escape, and by his nature he did not resist when free, so how will he escape after being bound, unless he says: the hamama released his way, making it a doer of good?
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation with two further points. First, Maturidi argues that if the nature of the lower (darkness) is to sink and the higher (light) to rise, then how could the lower (darkness) rise to attack light? This implies that darkness has both sinking and rising properties, which destroys the distinction that necessitated dualism. Second, if light naturally rises away from darkness, why did it not resist being imprisoned? The hamama (darkness's spirit) is depicted as active and powerful, while light is passive and weak. Maturidi asks: if light could not resist when free, how can it escape after being bound? The only way out is to claim that the hamama releases it, thereby making darkness the source of good (freeing light), which contradicts the Manichaean attribution of good to light and evil to darkness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument relies on empirical observation of light and darkness.
Correction: The argument is purely logical: it shows that the Manichaean assumptions about the natures of light and darkness lead to contradictions, regardless of empirical facts.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does Maturidi find in the Manichaean claim about the natures of light and darkness?
A) Light cannot be both infinite and finite
B) Darkness, by its nature, should sink, but it rose to attack light, contradicting its nature
C) The hamama is not sentient
D) Light is not eternal

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that the alleged natures of darkness and light lead to logical contradictions.

?

?
Maturidi's argument forces the dualist to concede that their own framework collapses. Have you ever been in a position where your own assumptions, when followed honestly, led you to an uncomfortable truth? How did you respond?

The Inversion of Good and Evil in Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi argues that if darkness perceives and imprisons light, then darkness possesses knowledge and power, which are goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Manichaeans' own narrative to expose the inconsistency of their dualistic classification of good and evil.

Core Idea
Dualism collapses when its own story attributes good to darkness and evil to light.

Maturidi continues his refutation of dualism by turning the Manichaeans' own creation story against them. In their account, darkness perceives light and subdues it. But if darkness sees and acts with purpose, it must possess knowledge and power—qualities they consider good. Meanwhile, light is left ignorant and helpless. Maturidi's argument is a masterful inversion: the narrative itself contradicts the moral labels attached to the two substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the contrast between the qualities of darkness and light.
2
Identify the premise
The passage begins with a conditional: if darkness saw and subdued light, then it possesses knowledge and sight. Mark this logical move.
3
List the attributed qualities
Make two columns: one for darkness's qualities (knowledge, sight, power, wealth, honor) and one for light's qualities (ignorance, incapacity, lowliness, humiliation).
Note that darkness also has 'domination' (qahr) which could be seen as positive or negative.
4
Reflect on the inversion
Ask yourself: if darkness has what Manichaeans call good and light has what they call evil, where does that leave their dualism?
Maturidi ends with sarcastic praise: 'How clear-sighted are you regarding good and evil!'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ، فإن جوهر الظلمة إن كان هو رأى النور وهو الذي أيس النور ليحبسه فهو الموصوف بالعلم والرؤية لا الذي لم يره ليتحصن منه ولم يعلم ما به يتخلص من قهره ، فإذا العلم والرؤية والمقدرة والغنى والشرف كله في جوهر الظلمة ، والقهر والجهل والعجز والذل والهوان في جوهر النور، فإن كان ذا كله خيرا والأول كله شرا فما أبصركم بالخير والشر .
And moreover, if the substance of darkness is the one that saw light and it is the one that subdued light to imprison it, then it is described by knowledge and sight, not the one that did not see it to protect itself from it and did not know how to escape from its domination. So if knowledge, sight, power, wealth, and honor are all in the substance of darkness, and domination, ignorance, incapacity, lowliness, and humiliation are in the substance of light, then if this (darkness's qualities) is all good and the former (light's qualities) is all evil, how clear-sighted are you regarding good and evil!
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage is the third argument, an inversion critique. Maturidi points out that in the Manichaean story, darkness (specifically the hamama) saw light and then subdued it. This implies that darkness possesses perception (ru'ya) and knowledge ('ilm), which are typically considered good qualities. Meanwhile, light is ignorant and helpless. Therefore, if we follow the Manichaean attribution of good and evil to substances, then darkness would be the source of good (knowledge, power, etc.) and light would be the source of evil (ignorance, weakness). This contradicts their fundamental moral classification, exposing an absurdity.

Misconception: Maturidi claims that darkness is actually good and light is actually evil.
Correction: Maturidi does not assert this; he uses the Manichaeans' own criteria to expose the inconsistency of their dualism. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Fourth Refutation: Dualism Collapses into Monism
Maturidi argues that if only darkness acts by choice, then all creation is from darkness, collapsing dualism into monism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the dualists' own premises to show that their system collapses into monism or infinite regress.

Core Idea
If only darkness creates, then the world is the work of one.

In this passage, Maturidi continues his refutation of dualism by examining the Manichaean claim that light acts by nature while darkness acts by choice. He demonstrates that if darkness alone is the creator, then the world is the product of a single agent, not two. Moreover, any attempt to preserve duality leads to an absurd multiplication of principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist premises
The Manichaeans claim that light acts by nature (passively) and darkness (the hamama) acts by choice (actively), and that the world is created by darkness.
This is their own starting point.
2
Identify the logical conclusion
If only darkness truly creates, then there is one effective agent, so the statement of duality is invalidated. The whole world is the act of the One.
3
Consider the alternative to salvage duality
If one tries to argue that light becomes another through its involvement, that leads to each part of the world being a separate principle, resulting in countless agents.
This absurd consequence reinforces the collapse into monism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك عندكم إن النور فعله طباع والهمامة فعلها اختيار ، والعالم أنشأه الحمامة ، بطل القول باثنين ، بل العالم كله فعل الواحد ، لكنه مزج أجزاءه بأجزاء الآخر : ولو كان الآخر بما يفعل به وفيه يصير آخر لتحصيل القول بالاثنين لكان كل من به وفيه العالم ، فيصير القول بما لا يُحصى عدده . ثم إذ كانت ذي طبع هو .
And likewise in your view: if the action of light is by nature and the action of the hamama is by choice, and the world was created by the hamama, then the statement of duality is invalidated; rather, the whole world is the act of the One, but it mixed its parts with parts of the other. And if the other, by what it does in it and through it, becomes another to achieve the statement of duality, then each of that by which and in which the world exists, so the statement becomes one that cannot be counted. Then if it is of a nature...
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage is the final argument preserved in the text. Maturidi points out that in the Manichaean system, light acts only by nature (passively), while darkness (the hamama) acts by choice (actively). The world is created by darkness (the hamama). Therefore, there is only one true agent—darkness—so the world is the work of a single entity, not two. If one tries to salvage duality by claiming that the other (light) becomes another through its involvement, then the number of principles multiplies indefinitely. The argument leads to the conclusion that the Manichaean position collapses into monism or infinite regress. The text cuts off, but the intended conclusion is that their own premises undermine dualism.

Correction: Maturidi is not endorsing monism; he is showing that the Manichaean logic forces either monism (one agent) or absurd multiplicity, thereby refuting their dualism.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that if only darkness is actively creative, then the world is necessarily the act of one being, not two.

The Contradiction of Mixture in Dualism
Maturidi argues against mixture in dualism via logical contradiction and metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the mixture of two opposing principles (e.g., light and darkness) is impossible because it requires each to act against its essential nature, thereby invalidating the dualistic framework.

Core Idea
Opposing natures cannot mix without self-contradiction.

Maturidi addresses a key claim of dualism: that two opposite principles (like light and darkness, or upper and lower) can mix to form the world. He argues that if their natures are fundamentally opposed—one always descends, the other always ascends—then any mixture would force one principle to act against its nature. This contradiction undermines the very basis for positing two distinct principles. A vivid metaphor of a dove and its captive reinforces the logical flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow and the sudden shift to the dove metaphor. The argument may seem dense because of unfamiliar terms, but focus on the main idea.
The passage contains some unclear words (e.g., 'ضعدا' likely 'صعدا') but the core logic is discernible.
2
Understand the initial logical argument
Identify the premise: it is the nature (ṭabʿ) of the lower to descend (tasafful) and the upper to ascend (ʿulūw). This mutual repulsion (tanāfur) defines them. Then Maturidi asks: how then can the lower ascend? If it does, it exhibits both properties, so the basis for two distinct principles collapses.
3
Analyze the dove metaphor
Maturidi likens the upper principle to a dove (ḥamāma) that imprisons the lower principle (like a horse). The lower, by nature, does not resist when released, so it cannot escape after being bound. The only escape would be if the dove itself frees it, making the dove the agent of good—an inconsistency in dualism.
Metaphor highlights the powerlessness of the lower principle and the contradiction in attributing both good and evil to separate principles.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how Maturidi uses both logic and metaphor to refute mixture. Ask yourself: What does this argument reveal about the necessity of a single Creator? The passage implicitly supports tawheed by showing dualism's internal contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن كان من طبع السفلى التسفل والعلوى العلو، وذلك معنى التنافر فكيف صار السفلى يذهب ضعدا، وذلك طبع العالى الصافي، وإليه مرجع وهو معنى العاقبة، الخير شها، فقد صار من السفلى الأمران جميعا، فبطل المعني الذي له لزم القول باثنين. ثم من العلوى النفار إلى العلوى، ولم يقم بوفاء ذلك ولا امتنع به عما كان بجوهر يتحدر حتى ارتفع عليه، وخلق العالم بحبسه، فكيف يطمعون أن يتخلص من يدى الحمامة، وهى مع ذلك حساسة فعالة بالخيل أوثقته / وقيدته وحبسته، وليست له قوة يتخلص [بها]، وبطبعه لم يمتنع عند التخلية، فكيف يتخلص بعد الوثاق إلا أن يقول : تخلى الهمامة سبيله، فيجعلها فاعلة الخير
Then if it is in the nature of the lower to descend and the upper to ascend, and that is the meaning of repulsion, how did the lower become ascending, while that is the nature of the pure upper, and to it is the return and it is the meaning of the end, [the] good [unclear], so both matters occurred from the lower, so the meaning for which the statement of two was necessary is invalidated. Then from the upper is aversion to the upper, and it did not fulfill that nor did it refrain from what was by essence descending until it rose above it, and it created the world by imprisoning it, so how can they hope that it will escape from the hands of the dove, while it is sensitive, active with the horse bound him and shackled him and imprisoned him, and it has no power to escape [by it], and by its nature it did not resist when released, so how can it escape after being shackled unless they say: the dove released its way, making it a doer of good.
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against dualism regarding the natures of the two principles (light and darkness, or upper and lower). The author states: if it is the nature of the lower to descend and the upper to ascend—this being the meaning of mutual repulsion (tanāfur)—then how can the lower become ascending, which is the nature of the pure upper? [The text contains some uncertain words, but the core argument is clear]. The lower would then exhibit both descending and ascending, invalidating the dualistic claim. The author then presents a metaphor of a dove (al-ḥamāma) that imprisons the other principle. The captured principle, by its nature, cannot resist when released, so how could it escape after being bound? The only way out is to claim that the dove itself frees it, making the dove the agent of good. This highlights an inconsistency in dualist thought.

Misconception: Dualism can explain mixture by allowing the two principles to blend without losing their essential natures.
Correction: Maturidi argues that if natures are opposite (one ascends, one descends), any mixture would force one to act against its nature, thus destroying the basis for distinguishing them.

Checkpoint

The Absurdity of Dualist Attributions of Good and Evil
Maturidi shows the absurdity of attributing knowledge to darkness and ignorance to light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a reductio ad absurdum to refute the dualist claim that light is good and darkness is evil.

Core Idea
If darkness imprisons light, then darkness has knowledge—contradicting its supposed evil.

Maturidi engages with a dualist worldview that associates light with good and darkness with evil. Through a simple hypothetical scenario, he turns this association on its head. By examining who possesses knowledge and power in the encounter between light and darkness, he exposes a logical contradiction that the dualist cannot escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The passage uses a rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the attributes being swapped
Maturidi lists attributes that would belong to darkness (knowledge, vision, power, wealth, honor) and to light (domination, ignorance, incapacity, lowliness, humiliation). Note which are considered good and which evil by dualists.
3
Understand the reductio
The dualist believes light is good and darkness is evil. But Maturidi shows that if darkness overcomes light, darkness must have knowledge (good), and light must have ignorance (evil). Thus, the dualist's classification is reversed — an absurd conclusion.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical punch
The final line sarcastically praises the dualist's discerning ability — a sharp condemnation of their flawed reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن جوهر الظلمة إن كان هو رأى النور وهو الذي أيس النور ليحبسه فهو الموصوف بالعلم والرؤية لا الذى لم يره ليتحصن منه ولم يعلم ما به يتخلص من قهره، فإذا العلم والرؤية والمقدرة والغنى والشرف كله في جوهر الظلمة، والقهر والجهل والعجز والذل والهوان فى جوهر النور، فإن كان ذا كله خيرا والأول كله شرا فما أبصركم بالخير والشر
And further, if the essence of darkness is the one that saw the light and it is the one that overcame the light to imprison it, then it is described with knowledge and vision, not the one that did not see it to fortify itself from it and did not know by what to escape from its domination. So knowledge, vision, power, wealth, and honor are all in the essence of darkness, while domination, ignorance, incapacity, lowliness, and humiliation are in the essence of light. If the latter is all good and the former all evil, then how perceptive are you of good and evil!
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the dualist association of light with good and darkness with evil. The author sets up a conditional: if darkness is the one that saw light and then overcame it to imprison it, then darkness must possess knowledge (ʿilm) and vision (ruʾya), while light did not see darkness and did not know how to escape. Consequently, all positive attributes—knowledge, vision, power, wealth, honor—reside in the essence of darkness, whereas the negative attributes—domination (qahr), ignorance (jahl), incapacity (ʿajz), lowliness (dhull), humiliation (hawān)—reside in the essence of light. But the dualists claim that light is good and darkness is evil. This reversal is absurd, and the author concludes with a rhetorical taunt: 'How perceptive you are of good and evil!'

الهوان (al-hawān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Light is inherently good and darkness inherently evil based on their essences.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if we examine their interaction, darkness would be the knowledgeable victor (good) and light the ignorant captive (evil), contradicting the inherent value attribution.

D) Both principles are equally good

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes the logical inconsistency in dualist attributions by showing that if darkness overcomes light, then darkness possesses knowledge and light ignorance — a direct contradiction of the dualist's value system.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Maturidi's Refutation of Dualism from Their Own Premises
Maturidi refutes dualism by analyzing light and darkness' natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the dualists' own claims about light and darkness to invalidate the necessity of two eternal principles.

Core Idea
If darkness creates by choice, dualism collapses into monotheism.

Maturidi here takes the arguments of the dualists (those who believe in two eternal principles, light and darkness) and turns them against themselves. The dualists claim that light acts by nature and darkness acts by choice, and that the world is created by darkness. Maturidi shows that if the world is the act of one agent (darkness), then dualism is false. He then exposes a further problem: if mixing parts creates a new entity, then to maintain dualism one would need endless principles. This lesson unpacks this crisp, devastating critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage has two main points: first, a direct refutation based on the world being the act of the dove; second, a problem with the mixing theory.
The last sentence is unclear due to OCR, but the core argument up to 'فيصير القول بما لا يُحصى عدده' is solid.
2
Identify the dualist premises
Identify what the dualists say: light acts by nature, darkness (the dove) acts by choice, and darkness creates the world by imprisoning light. Maturidi uses these to argue that the world is the act of one, not two.
3
Follow the first refutation
Maturidi says: if the world is created by darkness (the dove), then it is the act of one, so the claim of two principles is invalidated. This is a direct logical consequence from their own premise.
This is the main point: 'بطل القول باثنين'.
4
Examine the mixing argument
Maturidi then considers a possible dualist response: they might say that the world is a mixture of light and darkness. He argues that if mixing produces a new 'other' (third entity), then to maintain two principles one would need that the mixture contains the whole world, leading to an infinite regress of principles.
This is a deeper logical problem showing that dualism cannot be consistently maintained.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Think about why this refutation is powerful: it uses the dualists' own definitions to show that the world cannot be the product of two independent principles. This is a classic example of internal criticism in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك عندكم إن النور فعله طباع والهمامة فعلها اختيار، والعالم أنشأه الحمامة، بطل القول باثنين، بل العالم كله فعل الواحد، لكنه مزج أجزاءه بأجزاء الآخر : ولو كان الآخر بما يفعل به وفيه يصير آخر لتحصيل القول بالاثنين لكان كل من به وفيه العالم، فيصير القول بما لا يُحصى عدده . ثم إذ كانت ذي طبع هو .
Likewise with you: if light's action is nature and the dove's action is choice, and the world is created by the dove, then the statement of two is invalidated. Rather, the entire world is the act of the One, except that He mixed His parts with the parts of the other. And if the other, by what it does in it and through it, becomes another for the sake of establishing the statement of two, then each part would contain the world, leading to an uncountable number of statements.
Source pages p. 757

Commentary
This passage refutes dualism by examining the dualists' own premises. According to them, light (nūr) acts by nature (ṭabāʿ), while the dove (al-ḥamāma, representing darkness) acts by choice (ikhtiyār). The world is said to be created by the dove (darkness) by imprisoning light. Maturidi argues that this alone invalidates dualism: if the world is the act of the dove, then there is only one creator, but with a mixture of parts. He then raises an additional problem: if mixing parts produces a new 'other' (i.e., a third entity), then to maintain dualism one would need to claim that each part contains the world, leading to an infinite regress of principles. The final broken sentence likely relates to this.

Key Terms
طباع (ṭabāʿ)
اختيار (ikhtiyār)
فعل الواحد (fiʿl al-wāḥid)
مزج (mazj)
أجزاء (ajzāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dualism can be maintained by saying the world is a mixture of two distinct principles, each contributing equally.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if one principle (darkness) is the actual creator by choice, then it alone is the agent, invalidating two. Moreover, the mixture theory leads to infinite regress, so dualism is incoherent.

Checkpoint
According to the dualists' premises, how does darkness act?
A) By nature
B) By choice
C) By necessity
D) By chance

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by deriving a contradiction from the dualists' own statements: if darkness creates by choice, the world is the act of one, so dualism is false; the mixture theory leads to infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi shows that dualism self-destructs when its own premises are taken seriously. Are there areas in your own faith where you hold contradictory assumptions without realizing it? What would it take for you to examine them honestly today?
?
If darkness acts by choice, then it is a creator; but then the world is the act of one, so dualism fails. How often do you settle for a 'mixture' of truth and falsehood in your own beliefs, thinking you can hold both without contradiction? Will you let this argument force you to purify your tawheed?
?
The infinite regress argument warns against multiplying principles unnecessarily. In your life, are you adding unnecessary 'principles'—loyalties, attachments, fears—that compete with your devotion to the One? Will you cut them off today?

Why Light Cannot Escape Darkness: Maturidi's Refutation
Maturidi refutes dualist escape by infinite regress and contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the dualist concept of light escaping from darkness leads to logical absurdities, including infinite regress and contradiction.

Core Idea
Escape is meaningless: light cannot flee to an infinite void, and darkness pushing it would make evil do good.

Maturidi continues his critique of dualist cosmology by examining the escape mechanism. If light could escape darkness, how would that happen? He tests two possibilities and shows both collapse. This passage demonstrates the power of rational argument in refuting flawed theological systems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument. The text is dense; don't worry about details yet.
2
Identify the two escape scenarios
Notice that Maturidi presents two ways light could escape: by its own essence or by being pushed by darkness. List them.
These are the two 'possibilities' examined.
3
Analyze why each fails
For each scenario, see the specific reason Maturidi gives to show it is impossible. Pay attention to the infinite regress argument and the contradiction about darkness doing good.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظلمة هي التي بغت على النور ثم تخلص منها، فاما أن يكون التخلص منه؟ بالجوهر، وذلك محال؛ لأنه لم يمتنع منها به، مع ما يوجب تخلص أجزائه من حبس الهمامة وليس فيها علاه موضع يسير إليه ما انتزع منها؛ إذ غير هذا الجانب غير متناه، وهو بالتخليص يرجع إلى ما لا نهاية، فلا يجد لنفسه موضع قرار، فلا معنى للتخلص إلا أن يكون الظلمة تدفعه عن نفسها، فيكون دفعه خيرا إذ كان حبسه شرا. مع ما إذا دفع أجزاءه، وما علا ليس إلا أجزاؤه، فهو يدخل بعضه، / ولذلك نهاية، لكنه كان يحبسه فى جوهره ثم قهر كلية النور [٨١ب] فجعله سجنا لنفسه يحبس فيه عدوه، فيصير عدوه بجوهره حبيسا لنفسه. و بعد، فإن الظلمة ليس لها فى غير وجه الامتزاج حد، فهو إلى ماذا بالتخلص، فهو يبين أن لا معنى للتخلص، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Darkness is the one that attacked light and then [light] escaped from it. But either the escape is through essence? And that is impossible because it did not resist it thereby, along with what necessitates the escape of its parts from the imprisonment of the dove, while there is no place in it [?] to which what was extracted from it can go; since other than this side is infinite, and by liberation it returns to infinity, so it finds no place for itself to settle, so there is no meaning to escape unless it is that darkness pushes it away from itself, then its pushing away would be good since its imprisonment was evil. Moreover, when it pushes its parts, and what ascends is only its parts, so it enters some of it, and therefore it has an end, but it was imprisoning it in its essence, then overcame the entirety of light and made it a prison for itself in which it imprisons its enemy, so its enemy becomes by its essence imprisoned for itself. And further, darkness has no limit except in the aspect of mixture, so to what does it escape? This shows that there is no meaning to escape, and there is no power except by Allah.
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by examining the dualist notion of light escaping from darkness. Maturidi presents two possibilities: escape through essence (bi-l-jawhar) or through darkness pushing light away. The first is impossible because light's essence did not resist imprisonment originally. Moreover, if light's parts escape to an infinite space (since the side beyond darkness is infinite), they would have no place to settle, making escape meaningless. The second possibility (darkness pushing light) would mean darkness does good by releasing light, contradicting its evil nature. Additionally, if darkness pushes parts of light, those parts enter back into light, leading to an end (since light is finite? unclear). But then darkness imprisons light in its own essence, making light its own prisoner. Finally, darkness has no limit except when mixed, so escape is senseless. The passage ends with 'there is no power except by Allah,' affirming divine omnipotence.

Key Terms
التخلص (al-takhalluṣ)
الجوهر (al-jawhar)
اللامتناهي (al-lā mutanāhī)
القهر (al-qahr)
السجن (al-sijn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists claim that light can escape from darkness through its own nature or by being pushed.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both possibilities lead to logical absurdities: infinite regress or contradiction, so escape is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is one argument Maturidi gives against the possibility of light's escape from darkness?
A) Escape through essence is impossible because darkness has no essence
B) Light would have to escape to an infinite region with no resting place
C) Darkness would prevent escape by its power
D) Light does not want to escape

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the dualist idea of light escaping darkness is logically untenable, reinforcing the necessity of a single creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi shows that even if you imagine light escaping to an infinite void, it finds no resting place – it is lost. In your own life, where do you try to 'escape' to find peace or meaning apart from Allah? Are you fleeing to an infinite distraction that only leaves you unsettled? What would it mean to stop running and submit to the only One who gives true rest?
?
The argument forces you to see that a good act (release) coming from an evil source (darkness) would be contradictory. Examine your own heart: are there ways you justify actions by claiming good outcomes from impure intentions? How does this lesson call you to align your motives with the pure source of goodness, Allah?
?
At the end, Maturidi declares 'there is no power except by Allah.' This is not just a conclusion – it's a confession. In what area of your life are you acting as if you have the power to escape, to resist, or to control? Can you, right now, admit your helplessness and place your entire dependence on Allah alone?

The Imprisoned Light Cannot Be the Source of All Good
Maturidi critiques dualist claims about good and mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the dualist attribution of all good to an imprisoned light is self-contradictory and logically untenable.

Core Idea
Good cannot come from a source that is powerless and imprisoned.

In this passage, Maturidi exposes a fatal inconsistency in dualist theology. He challenges the claim that all good originates from the essence of light, pointing out that if light is overpowered and imprisoned by darkness, it cannot be the source of good. He also highlights the logical impossibility of two distinct essences mixing. This lesson unpacks his critique and shows why the dualist framework collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the tone of astonishment ('then amazing is their statement') and the two distinct criticisms that follow.
The text has OCR issues; read carefully.
2
Reflect on the first absurdity
Maturidi argues: if light is imprisoned and powerless, how can it produce all good? Consider the inherent contradiction in attributing goodness to a captive substance.
3
Examine the second contradiction
The dualists claim light and darkness are distinct essences, yet they also say they mix. Maturidi points out that distinct essences cannot mix – this is a logical impossibility.
Note the term 'tabāyun' (distinctness) and 'imitizāj' (mixture).
4
Understand the overall lesson
Maturidi's argument shows that dualism is incoherent. If their own descriptions of light and darkness lead to absurdities, then monotheism remains the rational alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ثم العجب من قولهم : إن الخير كله في العالم من جوهر النور، فمن أين يكون منه الخير وهو المقهور المحبوس ؟ والفعل كله الآخر ليحبسه به، فليس من النور غير البقاء في سجن الآخر ووثاقه، فمن يجنى منه خير ؟، إلا أن يرى ذلك من سائر الأجزاء التي لم تبغ عليه فيلقى أجزاءه في حبس آخر، وذلك هو الشر، وأنى لملك الخير ممنوع، وهو كله في الخلاص، وهو غير ثم التناقض. أنهم جعلوا التباين بالجوهر فمحال امتزاجها وهما بالجوهر متباينين وذلك قائم بحاله؛ إذ هم يرون الامتزاج غيرا . على أنه يقال لهم : الامتزاج
The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then amazing is their statement: 'All good in the world is from the essence of light.' So how can good come from it while it is overpowered and imprisoned? And the entire act of the other is to imprison it by it, so there is nothing from light except remaining in the prison of the other and its shackles. So who can derive good from it? Unless he sees that from the other parts that did not attack it, it casts its parts into another prison, and that is evil. How can the possessor of good be prevented, and it is entirely in deliverance, and it is not [?]. Then the contradiction: They made the distinction by essence, so their mixture is impossible because they are distinct in essence, and that stands as it is; when they see mixture as something else. Moreover, it is said to them: mixture...
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage, introduced by 'the Shaykh said' (likely an editorial comment from a transmitter), highlights two absurdities in dualism. First, they claim all good in the world comes from the essence of light, but according to their own account, light is overpowered (maqhūr) and imprisoned (maḥbūs). How can good issue from such a state? Light only remains in the prison and shackles of the other. Good cannot come from it unless one imagines that some parts of light (that did not attack) are cast into another prison, which is evil, so good remains unexplained. Second, there is a contradiction in claiming that the two essences are distinct (mutabāyinān) yet they mix. If they are distinct by essence, mixture is impossible, yet the dualists affirm mixture. The author then notes that the dualists consider mixture as something else (ghayr), but this only highlights the inconsistency. The passage ends abruptly with 'on that, it is said to them: mixture', implying the argument continues.

Key Terms
الخير (al-khayr)
المقهور (al-maqhūr)
المحبوس (al-maḥbūs)
التباين (al-tabāyun)
الامتزاج (al-imitizāj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism can coherently explain the coexistence of good and evil in the world.
Correction: Maturidi shows that dualism leads to contradictions: if light is the source of good but is imprisoned, it cannot produce good; and if light and darkness are distinct essences, they cannot mix. Therefore, dualism fails as a rational explanation.

Checkpoint

A) Good comes from both light and darkness equally
B) Light is the source of good but it is imprisoned and powerless
C) Darkness produces good by imprisoning light
D) Light is actually evil

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that the dualist claim of light as the source of all good collapses because light is depicted as helpless and the notion of mixing distinct essences is self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe Allah is the source of all good, but when you experience something good, do you sometimes forget His role and attribute it to your own effort or to causes you can control? How can you make tawheed more real in your daily gratitude?
?
This passage shows that attributing power to something other than Allah leads to contradictions. Are there any people, institutions, or things in your life that you unconsciously rely on as if they were independent sources of good? What would it cost you to break that reliance?
?
Maturidi is astonished at the dualists' inconsistency. What inconsistencies in your own beliefs about Allah's oneness have you allowed yourself to ignore? Will you commit to examine them honestly today?

Refuting Darkness Escaping Light
Refuting dualist claim that darkness can free itself from light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Maturidi argues that the dualist concept of darkness freeing itself from light is logically incoherent.

Core Idea
Darkness cannot free itself because infinite space provides no resting place.

This lesson examines one of the most sophisticated arguments in classical Islamic theology against the dualist (Manichaean) worldview, which posits two eternal principles of light and darkness. Imam Maturidi refutes the claim that darkness can rebel against light and then free itself, using pure logic to expose a fatal contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument. Notice the logical structure.
2
Understand the dualist claim
Identify the claim: darkness rebels and frees itself from light. This is the starting point of the refutation.
3
Analyze freeing by essence
Maturidi first examines freeing by essence (جوهر). He shows that freed parts would have no finite place to go, making freeing impossible.
4
Consider the alternative scenario
If darkness pushes light away, that leads to contradictions: infinite regress or finite boundaries, still meaningless.
5
Conclude the argument
The conclusion is that there is no meaning to freeing, affirming that all power is with Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الظلمة هي التي بغت على النور ثم تخلص منها، فاما أن يكون التخلص منه بالجوهر، وذلك محال؛ لأنه لم يمتنع منها به، مع ما يوجب تخلص أجزائه من حبس الهمامة وليس فيها علاه موضع يسير إليه ما انتزع منها؛ إذ غير هذا الجانب غير متناه، وهو بالتخليص يرجع إلى ما لا نهاية، فلا يجد لنفسه موضع قرار، فلا معنى للتخلص إلا أن يكون الظلمة تدفعه عن نفسها، فيكون دفعه خيرا إذ كان حبسه شرا. مع ما إذا دفع أجزاءه، وما علا ليس إلا أجزاؤه، فهو يدخل بعضه، ولذلك نهاية، لكنه كان يحبسه فى جوهره ثم قهر كلية النور فجعله سجنا لنفسه يحبس فيه عدوه، فيصير عدوه بجوهره حبيسا لنفسه. و بعد، فإن الظلمة ليس لها فى غير وجه الامتزاج حد، فهو إلى ماذا بالتخلص، فهو يبين أن لا معنى للتخلص، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Darkness is what that rebelled against light then freed itself from it. And the freeing can only be by essence, but that is impossible; because it did not prevent itself with it, along with what necessitates the freeing of its parts from the imprisonment of the [؟] and there is no place in it to which what is taken from it could go; since this side is infinite, and in freeing it returns to the infinite, so it finds no place of rest for itself. So there is no meaning to freeing unless darkness pushes it away from itself, and its pushing would be good since its imprisonment was evil. Moreover, when it pushes its parts, and what is above is only its parts, so it enters some of them, and that has an end, but it was imprisoning it in its essence then the entirety of light overcame it and made it a prison for itself in which it imprisons its enemy, so its enemy becomes by its essence a prisoner of itself. And further, darkness has no limit except in the face of mixing, so then towards what would it free? That shows that there is no meaning to freeing, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist (Manichaean) claim that darkness, after rebelling against light, can free itself. The argument proceeds by analyzing the process of freeing. If darkness frees itself by its own essence (جوهر), it is impossible because, given that space is infinite, the freed parts would have no place to settle; they would return to infinity and find no rest. Therefore, freeing would only be meaningful if darkness pushes the light away, but that would make the push good (since imprisonment is evil). However, if darkness pushes parts, those parts are still darkness, leading to infinite regress or finite boundaries. The conclusion is that darkness has no limit except when mixed with light, so freeing is meaningless. The argument uses the impossibility of infinite regress and the nature of finite bodies to show the incoherence of the dualist position.

Key Terms
ظلمة
نور
جوهر
تخلص
امتزاج
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Darkness can escape light as a physical process, like a substance moving away.
Correction: The argument is about metaphysical principles of good and evil, not physical light and darkness. Maturidi uses logical reasoning to show the incoherence of dualism.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi claim that darkness cannot free itself from light?
A) Because darkness is weaker than light
B) Because freed parts would have no finite place to settle in infinite space
C) Because darkness is inherently evil
D) Because light traps darkness completely

Key Takeaway
The dualist claim of darkness freeing itself is logically impossible because it leads to infinite regress and no finite place for freed parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever try to 'free' yourself from your own shortcomings through your own efforts, only to find yourself going in circles? Where do you truly find rest for your soul?
?
The passage shows that darkness has no power of its own. In what areas of your life are you still acting as if evil has the upper hand, ignoring that all power belongs to Allah?
?
If you believe that only Allah has true power, why do you still rely on your own strength to overcome your inner darkness? What would it look like to hand that struggle entirely to Him today?

Refutation of Good from Imprisoned Light
Refuting the dualist claim that good comes from imprisoned light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that all good originates from light by exposing an internal contradiction in their position.

Core Idea
If light is imprisoned, it cannot give good.

Dualists claimed that all good originates from a pure essence of light. But they also held that this light is imprisoned by darkness. Maturidi seizes on this contradiction to dismantle their argument. In this short but powerful passage, he shows that an imprisoned being cannot be the source of good.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice how Maturidi uses rhetorical questions.
2
Identify the dualist claim
Find the statement that all good comes from light. Then note that light is described as overpowered and imprisoned.
This is the premise Maturidi will challenge.
3
Follow the logical refutation
Observe how Maturidi questions the possibility of good coming from an imprisoned being. Then see his alternative: if some parts didn't rebel, they are cast into another prison, making evil.
The argument turns dualist logic against itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : ثم العجب من قولهم : إن الخير كله في العالم من جوهر النور، فمن أين يكون منه الخير وهو المقهور المحبوس ؟ والفعل كله الآخر ليحبسه به، فليس من النور غير البقاء في سجن الآخر ووثاقه، فمن يجنى منه خير ؟، إلا أن يرى ذلك من سائر الأجزاء التي لم تبغ عليه فيلقى أجزاءه في حبس آخر، وذلك هو الشر، وأنى لملك الخير
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Then amazing is their statement that all good in the world comes from the essence of light. So from where does good come from it while it is the overpowered and imprisoned one? And the action of the other is entirely to imprison it, so nothing remains of light except staying in the prison and shackles of the other. So who can harvest good from it? Unless he sees that from the other parts that did not rebel against it, so it throws its parts into another prison, and that is evil. And how [can this be] for the king of good?
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage presents a rhetorical refutation of the dualists who claim that all good in the world originates from the essence of light. Maturidi points out the contradiction: if light is imprisoned and overpowered by darkness, how can it produce good? The only possible good would be light's own parts that did not rebel, but then light throws those parts into another prison, which is evil. Thus, the very source of good is shown to be the source of evil, undermining their claim.

ظلمة
مقهور
محبوس

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good can originate from a being that is powerless and imprisoned.
Correction: Maturidi shows that an imprisoned being cannot be the source of good; it is contradictory to claim both that light is the source of all good and that it is overpowered.

B) The imprisoned light cannot produce good because it is powerless
C) Darkness is actually good

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that an imprisoned light cannot be the source of good, and its only other activity results in evil.

Contradiction in Dualist Claims of Mixing and Separation
Exposing the contradiction in dualists asserting both separation by essence and mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi exposes the logical contradiction in the dualist claim that light and darkness are separate yet can mix.

Core Idea
If two things are distinct by essence, mixing is impossible.

The dualist system asserts two eternal principles: light and darkness. They claim these are distinct by essence (جوهر) yet also capable of mixing. Imam Maturidi points out the inherent contradiction: if they are distinct in essence, they cannot mix; if they mix, then their distinctness is compromised. This short passage uses a clever logical dilemma to dismantle the dualist position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: تباين (distinction), جوهر (essence), امتزاج (mixing).
2
Identify the dualist claims
The dualists assert that light and darkness are distinct by essence (تباين بالجوهر) and also engage in mixing (امتزاج). Write down these two claims.
Maturidi considers these claims contradictory.
3
Follow the logical dilemma
Maturidi asks: Is mixing something that began after not existing? If yes, then mixing is a third principle. If no, then mixing is eternal. Both options lead to contradictions within dualism.
Work through the 'أليس بعد أن لم يكن' (Is it after it had not been?) question.
4
Understand the final point
Maturidi then asks whether mixing is light or darkness. If mixing could be both, then light and darkness could coexist, which the dualists deny. This completes the exposure of inconsistency.
The phrase 'ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a typical closing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التناقض. أنهم جعلوا التباين بالجوهر فمحال امتزاجها وهما بالجوهر متباينين وذلك قائم بحاله؛ إذ هم يرون الامتزاج غيرا. على أنه يقال لهم: الامتزاج أليس بعد أن لم يكن؟ أو غيرهما؟ فإن قال: بالأولين، أحال؛ لأنه أثبت الامتزاج والتباين لنفسه لا بد من: بلى. قيل: آكان هو النور أو الظلمة. ولو جاز ذلك الجاز وجودهما معا، وهو بين، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the contradiction: they made distinction by essence, so their mixing is impossible while they are distinct by essence, and that remains as it is; since they see mixing as something else. Moreover, it is said to them: Is mixing after it had not been? Or something else? If they say: by the two [previous] possibilities, it is impossible; because he would have established mixing and distinction for the same thing. There is no alternative but: yes. It was said: Was it the light or the darkness? And if that were allowed, then both could exist together, and that is clear, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 758

Commentary
This passage exposes an internal contradiction in the dualist system. The dualists claim that light and darkness are distinct by essence (جوهر), but they also claim that mixing (امتزاج) occurs between them. Maturidi argues that if they are distinct by essence, mixing is impossible. He then presents a dilemma: if mixing occurs, did it begin after not existing? If they affirm that, they imply that mixing is something separate, leading to a third principle. If they deny that mixing began, then mixing is eternal, contradicting their view that light and darkness are separate. The argument forces them to admit either light or darkness as the source of mixing, but if both can mix, they could coexist, which undermines their dualism.

Key Terms
تباين
جوهر
امتزاج
نور
ظلمة
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe that light and darkness can be distinct in essence and also mix with each other.
Correction: If two things are distinct in essence, they cannot mix. Claiming both is logically contradictory, as Maturidi shows.

Checkpoint
What contradiction does Maturidi point out in the dualist position?
A) They claim light and darkness are separate yet can mix
B) They claim mixing is eternal
C) They claim darkness is good
D) They claim both are uncreated

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi exposes the logical impossibility of dualism by showing that the claim of distinct essences is incompatible with the claim of mixing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi exposes a contradiction in the dualists' worldview. Reflect on your own beliefs: Is there any inconsistency between what you profess about Allah and how you live your daily life? Commit to identifying one area today and correcting it.
?
The dualists believed in two equal eternal principles. This lesson shows that such a belief leads to logical absurdity. Have you ever held onto a belief or habit that, upon honest reflection, contradicts pure Tawheed? What step will you take this week to align your inner convictions with the oneness of Allah?
?
The argument forces the dualist to admit a third principle or eternal coexistence. How carefully do you examine the logical consequences of your own thoughts and actions? Pledge to spend five minutes tonight reviewing one of your attitudes to ensure it is free from hidden contradictions.
