Maturidi's Refutation of Dualism: The Regress Argument
Maturidi refutes dualism by regress arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes dualism by showing that it leads to an infinite regress of principles, and why the dualist position collapses under its own logic.

Core Idea
Dualism inevitably multiplies principles until contradiction.

Maturidi confronts the dualist claim that good and evil originate from two independent principles. He demonstrates that this claim cannot stand because it forces an endless chain of additional principles. Through a series of logical steps, he shows that dualism ultimately defeats itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The text is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first regress
Notice how Maturidi starts with the dualist assumption of two principles. He argues that if two, then four mutually conflicting principles must follow. Identify this step.
The principle of consistency forces multiplication.
3
Trace the alternative regress
Look at the alternative: if one says four (like natures), then six (directions), then a seventh (bearer), or five (substratum). Follow the logic of these enumerations.
Each step is based on inherent properties of the posited principles.
4
Understand the dualist's core claim
Return to the dualist's original two principles. Maturidi argues that if they are separate (mutable), they cannot mix without a third principle. Grasp why this is fatal.
Mixing requires a common agent or medium.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final sentence: 'Thus their statement is invalidated from the direction they sought to prove it.' Understand that dualism's own logic leads to its refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما لو كان لذينك الوجهين يجب القول بالاثنين ليجب القول بالأربع متضادة ، كل يضر الكل ، ولو كان بهذا يقول بالأربع : نحو الطبائع ؛ إذ هى ليجب القول بالست ؛ بما لا يخلو شيء قائم عن جهات ست ، وذلك يوجب القول بالسابع ؛ لما كان حامل تلك الجهات لا يوصف بجهة سابتها ، أو بالخمس بما كان الذي فيه اجتماع تلك الطبائع هو الخامس ، لا يوصف بحر ولا يرد ولو كان كما تقول الثنوية ليجب القول بالثالث ؛ لما كانا ولم يكن العالم ، ولا : ، ولا شر ، ومحال كون متبائن بنفسه ، ممتزحا بنفسه لا يوجب الاجتماع والتناقض ثبت كون ذلك بغيرهما ، و به كان كل خير وشر ، فيبطل قولهم من حيث راموا اثباته.
Moreover, if for those two aspects it is necessary to posit two, then it would be necessary to posit four mutually conflicting [principles], each harming all. And if one were to say four: like the natures (tabai'), then it would be necessary to posit six, because nothing that subsists is free from six directions, and that necessitates positing a seventh, because the bearer of those directions is not described by a seventh direction; or [to posit] five, because that in which those natures come together is the fifth, not described by motion or rest. And if it were as the dualists say, it would be necessary to posit a third, because they were two while the world did not exist, and neither : nor evil. It is impossible for something self-distinct to be self-mixing without necessitating combination and contradiction; so it is established that that [mixing] is from something else, and by it every good and evil exists. Thus their statement is invalidated from the direction they sought to prove it.
Source pages p. 764

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualism by the method of reductio ad absurdum. The author argues that if one posits two eternal principles (as dualists do), then by the same logic one would be forced to posit four (mutually conflicting), then six (based on directions), then a seventh (the bearer of directions), or five (the substratum of natures). Then he returns to the dualist claim: if there were only two before the world, they must be separate; mixing without a third is impossible, so a third is needed. Thus their attempt to explain good and evil by two sources fails.

Key Terms
ثنوية
طبائع
جهات
متبائن
ممتزج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The regress argument is a mere logical game without real theological force.
Correction: On the contrary, it is a rigorous demonstration that dualism cannot account for the unity of the world and leads to internal contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows from the dualist claim of two principles?
A) A third principle is necessary for mixing
B) Four principles are necessary
C) The two principles are sufficient
D) Only one principle exists

Key Takeaway
Dualism fails because it cannot logically stop at two principles; it inevitably requires more, undermining its own foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever subconsciously attribute the good in your life to your own efforts and the bad to external circumstances, as if there are two separate sources? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that and call you to a more unified reliance on Allah alone?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt torn between two opposing desires or loyalties. Maturidi shows that two distinct principles cannot combine on their own—what does that teach you about the need for a single, supreme authority (Allah) to bring harmony to your inner conflicts?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah creates both good and evil, how should that change the way you react to trials and blessings? Maturidi's logical refutation of dualism removes any excuse to attribute events to independent sources—so what step will you take this week to consciously attribute every single occurrence to Allah?

Refutation of the Light-Darkness Mixture Theory
Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that the world results from mixing light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational refutation of the dualist theory that the world originates from a mixture of light and darkness, and why he considers it logically incoherent.

Core Idea
If light is pure and self-subsistent, it cannot be mixed with and overpowered by darkness.

The dualists claimed that the world arose from a mixture of light and darkness, with darkness veiling and conquering light. Maturidi exposes the absurdity of this view by pointing out that light, being pure and self-sufficient, cannot be subject to mixture and defeat. This passage is a concise demonstration of how rational critique dismantles false cosmologies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical style and the key terms.
The Arabic includes some textual ambiguity; the translation clarifies Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the dualist claim
Extract the dualists' assertion: the world is a mixture of light and darkness, every part of light is mixed with darkness, and darkness veils and conquers light.
This sets up the target of refutation.
3
Analyze Maturidi's critique
Note the two main points: first, how can light, being self-subsistent and pure, be in distress and ignorance? Second, if light's parts are captured by darkness, how can it hope to be released? This shows an internal contradiction.
Maturidi uses rhetorical questions to expose the flaw.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider that the dualist theory undermines the transcendence and sovereignty of the true source of good (Allah). Maturidi's refutation protects divine oneness.
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ زعموا أن العالم إنما هو امتزاج النور والظلمة ، فما من جزء من أجزاء النور إلا هو مشوب بجزء من أجزاء الظلمة ، والظلمة هي الساترة ثم الساترة ثم هى القاهرة للنور ، فما من خير يرجى بدوه منه إلا والظلمة تقهره وتستره عن التجلي لأهل المذهب ، فأنى لهم والعلم والوقوف على طريق الحكمة ، حتى يدعون في الآخر دعوى بشر والعجب أن نورهم - مع قيامه بنفسه وصفائه عن شوائب الظلمة لم يعلم [ما] عليه الامتزاج من الضنك والضيق ومن الجهل والعجز ، ثم يرجى" يجزء منه عند خروجه عن جوهره ووقوعه فى يدى عدوه أنه يطلقه ، على الحكمة التي هو عند تمامه
For they claimed that the world is only a mixture (imtizaj) of light (nur) and darkness (zulma). There is no part of the parts of light except that it is mixed with a part of the parts of darkness, and darkness is the veiler, then the veiler, and it is the conqueror of light. So there is no good hoped for from it except that darkness overcomes it and veils it from manifestation to the people of the sect. So how can they have knowledge and standing upon the path of wisdom, until they claim in the end the claim of a human? And the wonder is that their light – despite its subsistence by itself and its purity from the blemishes of darkness – did not know what mixture it was in of distress and narrowness, of ignorance and incapacity. Then it is hoped that a part of it, when it exits from its essence and falls into the hands of its enemy, will release it, according to the wisdom which is at its completion?

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of the dualist cosmology. Maturidi points out the inconsistency: if light is pure, self-subsistent, and inherently good, how can it be mixed with and overcome by darkness? Darkness is described as a veiler and conqueror, which contradicts the sovereignty of light. Moreover, the idea that a part of light, when captured by darkness, can be released is absurd because light, being pure, should not be subject to such constraints. The dualists' claim thus lacks wisdom.

Correction: Maturidi shows the theory is internally inconsistent: light cannot be both pure and self-subsistent yet be mixed with and overcome by darkness, revealing the theory lacks wisdom.

Maturidi's Refutation of Dualism
Maturidi refutes the dualist notion of two essences by showing its logical inconsistency regarding wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument against the dualist claim of two co-eternal essences and why it leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Dualism about good and evil collapses under logical scrutiny.

Maturidi tackles one of the oldest theological challenges: the problem of evil. Some argued for two co-eternal principles, one good and one evil. In this passage, Maturidi shows that such a view cannot consistently attribute wisdom to both principles, making the entire system self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key terms: جوهرين (two essences), شر وخير (evil and good), حكمة (wisdom).
The Arabic is corrupt; rely on the translation and commentary.
2
Understand the dualist claim
Dualists posit two essences: one of light/good and one of darkness/evil. They claim that both are eternal and active.
3
Follow Maturidi's critique
Maturidi argues that if both essences are claimed to be wise, then their judgments would be identical, making speech meaningless. Alternatively, if one is wise and the other ignorant, then the ignorant one cannot be a source of wisdom.
This shows the logical incoherence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that such speech is absurd. The only source of power and wisdom is Allah.
This reinforces tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأحق من لا يدعى الحكمة ولا يناظر أهانها ولا يشرع إلى جوهرين عند نفسه : شر وخير، وكذا كل لم يبلغها فيها التنوى؛ لأنه يرجع أحد عندهم جوهر... وأما إن كان الشرع فيها بجوشر النور وكذلك من يكلمه فيها، فها حكمان لا يخفى عليهما شيء، لا معنى لكليهما، وهما بأنفسهما ذلك، أو، أو، ومحال احتمالهما الحكمة، أو أحدهما جوهر النور والآخر لا يحتمل ذا الجهل ولا الآخر العلم، فيكون التكلم عبثا لا معنى له، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And more deserving are those who do not claim wisdom, nor argue with its people, nor proceed to two essences (jawharayn) in themselves: evil and good. And likewise everyone who has not reached it in the dualism, because one of them returns? to an essence with them... But if the law in it is by the essence of light, and likewise the one who speaks in it, then here are two judgments that nothing is hidden from them, no meaning for both, and they are in themselves that, or, or, and it is impossible for them to bear wisdom, or one of them is the essence of light and the other does not bear ignorance nor the other knowledge, so the speech becomes absurd with no meaning. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 764

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the dualist notion of two essences, one good and one evil. Maturidi argues that if the dualists claim wisdom for their system, they cannot consistently assign properties to the two essences. If they say the law is based on the essence of light, and someone speaks about it, then there are two judgments that are identical, which is meaningless. It is impossible for both essences to bear wisdom, because one (light) would be associated with knowledge and the other (darkness) with ignorance, leading to absurdity. Their discourse is therefore futile.

Key Terms
جوهرين
شر وخير
حكمة
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism (two co-eternal principles) can explain the existence of good and evil without logical contradictions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if both essences are wise, they would be identical in judgment, leading to nonsense. If one is wise and the other ignorant, then the ignorant one cannot be a source of wisdom, so the system fails.

Checkpoint

A) They cannot explain the origin of the world
B) They cannot consistently assign wisdom to both essences
C) They deny the existence of evil
D) They claim the essences are material

Key Takeaway
Dualism is logically incoherent because it cannot consistently attribute wisdom to two opposing eternal principles; thus, only Allah, the One, is the source of all wisdom and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Refutation of Dualist Belief about Light and Darkness
Maturidi refutes dualist claim about light and darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the dualist claim that light and darkness are two independent eternal principles by exposing internal contradictions.

Core Idea
The dualist view of light and darkness collapses because light cannot mix with darkness without losing its purity.

Imam Maturidi here addresses a specific sect that believed light and darkness are two eternal, self-subsistent principles. He points out that if light is truly pure and self-subsistent, it cannot be affected by darkness. The dualist's hope that light will be released from darkness contradicts its claimed nature. This lesson examines the logical flaw in the dualist argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The Arabic text has some OCR issues, so refer to the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the contradiction
Notice how Maturidi points out that if light is self-subsistent and pure, it cannot mix with darkness without becoming constrained and ignorant. Identify this main inconsistency.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Understand the conclusion
Reflect on Maturidi's conclusion that the dualist position leads to absurdity and that speech about two essences is meaningless. See how this undermines the entire dualist framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعجب أن نورهم - مع قيامه بنفسه وصفائه عن شوائب الظلمة لم يعلم [ما] عليه الامتزاج من الضنك والضيق ومن الجهل والعجز ، ثم يرجى" يجزء منه عند خروجه عن جوهره ووقوعه فى يدى عدوه أنه يطلقه ، على الحكمة التي هو عند تمامه ، وأحق من لا يدعى الحكمة ولا يناظر أهانها ولا يشرع إلى جوهرين عند نفسه : شر وخير ، وكذا كل لم يبلغها فيها التنوى ؛ لأنه يرجع أحد عنده عندهم جوهر وأما إن كان الشرع فيها بجوشر النور وكذلك من يكلمه فيها ، فها حكمان لا يخفى عليهما شيء ، لا معنى لكليهما ، وهما بأنفسهما ذلك ، أو ، ومحال احتمالهما الحكمة ، أو أحدهما جوهر النور والآخر لا يحتمل ذا الجهل ولا الآخر العلم ، فيكون التكلم عبثا لا معنى له ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And it is astonishing that their light - despite its self-subsistence and purity from the impurities of darkness - it is not known [what] is upon it of mixture from constriction and straitness and from ignorance and incapacity. Then it is hoped that a part of it when it leaves its essence and falls into the hands of its enemy, that it will release it, according to the wisdom which it is at its completion. And more deserving is one who does not claim wisdom and does not argue and does not honor it, and does not proceed to two essences in himself: evil and good. And likewise everyone who did not reach it in [؟]. Because he returns one with them to an essence. And if the law in it is by the essence of light, and likewise whoever speaks about it, then here are two judgments, nothing is hidden from them, no meaning for both of them, and they are by themselves that, or, and impossible for them to bear wisdom, or one of them is the essence of light and the other does not bear the ignorance nor the other knowledge, so the speech becomes nonsense with no meaning, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 765

Commentary
This passage refutes the dualist claim that light and darkness are two eternal, independent principles. Maturidi begins by pointing out an inconsistency: if light is pure and self-subsistent, how does it mix with darkness and become constrained, ignorant, and weak? The dualist then hopes that when light leaves its essence and falls into the hands of its enemy (darkness), it will be released, which is contrary to the wisdom it supposedly has. Maturidi argues that one who does not claim wisdom is better than the dualist who dishonors wisdom. The dualist posits two essences: good and evil. But if we consider the law (shar‘) regarding light and darkness, there are two judgments that are either identical (and thus meaningless) or contradictory. They cannot both incorporate wisdom, because if one is the essence of light and the other cannot admit ignorance or knowledge, then speaking about them is vain. Thus the dualist position leads to absurdity.

Misconception: Light and darkness are two eternal, independent principles with equal power.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this belief leads to logical contradictions, such as light mixing with darkness and losing its purity, which undermines its claimed self-subsistence.

The Debate of Ja'far ibn Harb with a Dualist
Maturidi recounts a debate showing the dualist's logical inconsistency, leading to conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses a narrative debate to expose the logical inconsistency of dualism through the scenario of wrongdoing and apology.

Core Idea
Dualism collapses when tested against real human interactions.

This passage presents a vivid debate between Ja'far ibn Harb and a dualist. Through a simple scenario of wrongdoing and apology, Ja'far forces the dualist to confront the absurd consequences of believing in two separate sources of good and evil. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the scenario: a killer wrongfully kills someone, then apologizes and admits wrongdoing.
Notice that the dualist is forced to admit the victim is 'better' because the killer apologized.
2
Identify the dualist's trap
Ja'far compels the dualist to acknowledge that if the killer's essence were from a different source (light vs. darkness), the apology would be a lie from the light, thus evil.
This shows the internal contradiction: the light source would be producing evil.
3
Examine the leader's response
The dualist leader compares the apology to someone hurting his beast and then apologizing to it, implying the apology is just an expression of pain.
4
Understand Ja'far's rebuttal
Ja'far counters that such an apology is either ignorance (if genuine) or merely a gesture of reconciliation. The leader's analogy actually supports Ja'far's point.
The dualist then converts, acknowledging the argument's force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر جعفر بن حربا أنه سأل ثنويا عمن قتل آخر ظلما ثم اعتذر إليه وأقر بالإساءة ، فألزمه أن الثانى خير ، ولو كان من غيره جوهر الأول كان كذبا من النور وهو شر . فكتب ذلك إلى رئيس لهم، فكتب الرئيس مجيبا : إن ذلك كمن ينفح دابته ويعتذر هو . فقال جعفر : إنما ذلك توجع منه ، ولو اعتذر في الحقيقة كان جاهلا ، إلا أن يكون الاعتذار من تقريبه الدابة إليه فأسلم الرجل ، وحق له أن يسلم ، وما ذكر ابن حرب لازم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And Ja'far ibn Harb mentioned that he asked a dualist about one who killed another unjustly, then apologized to him and admitted wrongdoing, and he compelled him that the second person is better, and if the essence of the first were from other than him, it would be a lie from the light and it is evil. So he wrote that to a leader of theirs, and the leader wrote in reply: 'That is like one who hurts his beast and then apologizes to it.' Ja'far said: 'That is only pain from him, and if he apologized in reality, he would be ignorant, unless the apology is from bringing the beast near to him.' So the man converted, and he deserved to convert. And what Ibn Harb mentioned is binding, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 768

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote illustrating a logical refutation of dualism. Ja'far ibn Harb asks a dualist about a scenario: someone kills another unjustly, then apologizes and admits wrongdoing. Ja'far forces the dualist to conclude that the second person (the victim) is better (because he was wronged and received apology). But if the first person's essence were from a source other than the second, then the apology would be a lie from the light, and thus evil. The dualist writes to their leader, who replies with a comparison: it is like someone who hurts his beast and then apologizes to it—meaning the apology is merely an expression of pain, not a true apology. Ja'far rebuts that this interpretation only shows the person's own distress; if it were a true apology, the apologizer would be ignorant (since the beast cannot understand), unless the apology is merely bringing the beast near (an act of reconciliation). The dualist then converts, and Maturidi affirms that Ja'far's argument is binding. This shows the internal inconsistency of dualist thought: if good and evil come from two different principles, then true apology and accountability become incoherent.

ثنوي

الاعتذار
النور
الشر
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualism can logically account for good and evil interactions in human relationships.
Correction: This passage shows that dualism leads to absurdities: if wrong acts come from a source of evil, then an apology (from the same source) would be insincere or contradictory, making moral accountability impossible.

Checkpoint

A) He admitted defeat.
B) He compared the scenario to someone hurting his beast and then apologizing to it.
C) He claimed the victim deserved death.
D) He said the killer was from the light.

Key Takeaway
The dualist's own admission that the victim is 'better' and that the apology would be a lie from the light exposes the incoherence of dualism, leading to the dualist's conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ja'far's argument forced the dualist to convert because he could not bear the logical inconsistency. Is there a belief or habit in your life that you cling to even though it is internally inconsistent with your faith? What would it take for you to admit that inconsistency and change?

Why the Mu'tazila View on Evil Leads to Dualism
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila concession on evil leads them to dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila denial of real evil in creation paradoxically supports the dualist position.

Core Idea
Denying evil's reality opens the door to dualism.

Maturidi here tackles a critical weakness in the Mu'tazila framework. They claim that evil is not truly from Allah but only called so metaphorically. Maturidi argues that this concession, when debating dualists, backfires: it forces them into affirming that evil must come from a different source, thus undermining tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let Maturidi's argument unfold naturally.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim about evil
Find where the Mu'tazila say there is no evil in Allah's creation, only metaphorically. Note that they deny the reality of evil.
This is the starting point of Maturidi's critique.
3
Understand Maturidi's critique of their debate method
Maturidi explains that the Mu'tazila try to remove evil from being evil in debates with dualists. He then points out that if they concede evil exists (even metaphorically), they cannot consistently argue that all comes from one God.
Look for the phrase 'فهو محال فاسد' (it is impossible and corrupt).
4
See the consequence: dualism
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila position ends up supporting the dualist claim that evil comes from a different source than good. This forces them into 'monotheism through dualism' (التوحيد بالتثنية).
This is the key insight: denying real evil leads to affirming two creators.
5
Connect to the broader issue of God's creation of acts
The passage ends by noting that the Mu'tazila denied God's creation of servants' acts, including sins and evils, so they were contradicted by the reality of evil's creation.
This links the discussion to the broader kalām topic of human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة على قول المعتزلة خطأ؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن ليس في خلق الله شر وإنما سمى شرا بالمجاز، فإنما طريق مناظرتهم الثنوية فى إزالة ما ظنوه شرا أن يكون شرا، فأما أن يسلموا الثنوية ويلزمهم القول بالخالق الواحد من الوجه الذي يوجد من غير الله تعالى الوجهين جميعا ويجعلونه على الصانع فيجد القول بنفى ذلك، فهو محال فاسد؛ لما فيه تثبيت معرفته وتوحيده يخلق الشر والخير ثم ينفى أحدهما الحقيقة، رجعت إلى قول الثنوية بأن الذى منه خلق الشر في الحقيقة غير الذي منه خلق الخير، فيلزمه التوحيد بالتثنية، ووجه قولهم في هذا أنهم أنكروا خلق أفعال العباد بما فيها السيئات والمعاصى والشرور، فعورضوا بخلق الشرور
The issue is wrong according to the Mu'tazila; for it is from their doctrine that there is no evil in Allah's creation, but it is called evil metaphorically. Thus the method of their debate with the dualists is to remove what they thought to be evil from being evil. As for them conceding to the dualists and compelling them to affirm the single Creator from the aspect that exists from other than Allah ? both aspects and they make it upon the Maker, then they find the statement denying that—it is impossible and corrupt; because it contains establishing His knowledge and His oneness: He creates evil and good, then He denies one of them in reality. It returns to the dualists' statement that the one from whom evil is created in reality is other than the one from whom good is created, so he is compelled to monotheism through dualism. And the point of their statement in this is that they denied the creation of the acts of servants, including the bad deeds, sins, and evils, so they were opposed by the creation of evils.
Source pages p. 768

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazila position on evil. Maturidi first states that the Mu'tazila position is wrong because they hold that there is no genuine evil in Allah's creation—evil is only called so metaphorically. Their method of debating dualists, therefore, is to try to remove what the dualists mistakenly consider evil from being truly evil. But Maturidi points out that if the Mu'tazila concede to the dualists the existence of evil (even metaphorically) and then try to compel them to affirm one Creator by arguing that both good and evil come from Him, they fall into an impossibility. Why? Because by affirming that God creates both good and evil, and then denying that one of them is real, they end up supporting the dualist claim: that the source of evil is different from the source of good. This forces the Mu'tazila into a form of monotheism that is actually dualism (tawhid through tathniya). The passage concludes by noting that the Mu'tazila's error originates in their denial that God creates the acts of servants, including sins and evils; thus they were contradicted by the reality of the creation of evils (a point the text does not fully elaborate due to the page ending).

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الشر
المجاز
خلق أفعال العباد
السيئات
المعاصي
التوحيد بالتثنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That denying the reality of evil solves the problem of evil and avoids attributing evil to God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this denial actually leads to dualism, because if evil is conceded even metaphorically, it forces a separate origin for evil, undermining tawhid.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say is the consequence of the Mu'tazila conceding that God creates evil?
A) It proves monotheism.
B) It forces them into affirming that evil comes from a different source than good, i.e., dualism.
C) It has no effect.
D) It shows that evil is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila attempt to avoid attributing evil to God by calling it metaphorical actually leads to the dualist heresy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face evil or suffering in your life, do you tend to downplay it as 'not really evil' to avoid discomfort with God's decree? How might that subtle denial slowly erode your reliance on tawhid?
?
Maturidi warns that denying the reality of evil leads to dualism. Is there any area in your life where you subtly attribute evil to a source other than Allah—such as blaming circumstances, people, or yourself—instead of acknowledging it as from Him while still trusting His perfect wisdom?
?
Think of a recent sin or trial you experienced. Did you find yourself making excuses or redefining it to protect your image of God? What would it cost you to honestly say, 'This comes from Allah, and He is all-good,' and how would that change your response to hardship?

Ja'far ibn Harb's Refutation of Dualism
Ja'far ibn Harb debates dualist on good/evil via murderer's apology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ja'far ibn Harb used the example of a murderer's apology to logically refute the dualist belief that good and evil come from separate eternal substances.

Core Idea
Good and evil can emerge from a single source, disproving dualism.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi recounts a debate between the Mu'tazilite scholar Ja'far ibn Harb and a dualist. The dualist held that good flows only from light and evil only from darkness. Ja'far presents a scenario that forces the dualist to admit a contradiction, leading to his conversion. This story illustrates how kalam uses logical reasoning to defend monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the narrative. Notice the key players: Ja'far, the dualist, and the dualist leader.
Pay attention to the logical structure hidden in the story.
2
Identify the dualist's premise
Understand that the dualist believes light (جوه النور) produces only good, and darkness produces only evil. This is the foundation of his worldview.
Ja'far will attack this separation.
3
Analyze Ja'far's argument
Ja'far presents a murderer who kills unjustly (evil) and then apologizes (good). He forces the dualist to consider the source of the apology: if it comes from the same substance as the murder, then that substance produces both good and evil (contradicting dualism). If it comes from a different substance (light), then light produces a lie (evil), also contradicting.
The dualist's response via their leader shows a misunderstanding.
4
Reflect on the leader's response and conversion
The leader compares the apology to beating a donkey and then apologizing. Ja'far clarifies that this is not a genuine apology but an expression of pain. The dualist realizes his error and converts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر جعفر بن حربا أنه سأل ثنويا عمن قتل آخر ظلما ثم اعتذر إليه وأقر بالإساءة، فألزمه أن الثانى خير، ولو كان من غيره جوهر الأول كان كذبا من النور وهو شر. فكتب ذلك إلى رئيس لهم، فكتب الرئيس مجيبا: إن ذلك كمن ينفح دابته ويعتذر هو . فقال جعفر: إنما ذلك توجع منه، ولو اعتذر في الحقيقة كان جاهلا، إلا أن يكون الاعتذار من تقريبه الدابة إليه فأسلم الرجل، وحق له أن يسلم، وما ذكر ابن حرب لازم، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And Ja'far ibn Harb mentioned that he asked a dualist about someone who killed another unjustly, then apologized to him and admitted wrongdoing. He (Ja'far) made him (the dualist) realize that the second (the apology) is good, and if it had come from other than the substance of the first, it would be a lie from the light, and it is evil. So that was written to a leader of theirs, and the leader wrote back: 'That is like one who beats his donkey and then apologizes.' Ja'far said: 'That is only an expression of pain from him, and if he truly apologized he would be ignorant, unless the apology is from bringing the donkey close to him.' So the man converted to Islam, and it is right that he converted. And what Ibn Harb mentioned is necessary, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 768

Commentary
This passage presents a story: Ja'far ibn Harb, a Mu'tazilite scholar, debates a dualist. The dualist's position is that good comes only from light, evil only from darkness. Ja'far poses a case: a murderer kills unjustly, then apologizes. The apology is good. But if the apology comes from a source other than the murderer's substance (which is from darkness), it would be a lie from the light—meaning the light would produce a lie, which is evil. Thus, the dualist's own logic forces him to admit that the same person (the murderer) can produce both good and evil, or that the apology is from the same substance as the murder. The dualist's leader compares the situation to beating a donkey and then apologizing, which Ja'far notes is merely an expression of pain, not a true apology. The dualist then converts to Islam. The passage ends by affirming that Ibn Harb's argument is sound.

Key Terms
ثنوية
جوهر النور
اعتذار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dualist believes that good and evil come from two separate eternal substances (light and darkness) that never mix. Good can only come from light, evil only from darkness.
Correction: Ja'far shows that the same person can produce both good and evil acts, meaning good and evil can originate from a single source, undermining the dualist's separation.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does Ja'far's scenario create for the dualist?
A) The murderer cannot apologize because he is evil.
B) The apology is evil because it comes from a murderer.
C) Light would have to produce a lie (evil) if the apology comes from light.
D) The dualist's leader gave a weak analogy.

Key Takeaway
Ja'far's argument shows that if the apology is from a different source it becomes a lie from light, contradicting the dualist's own principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You, like the dualist, may try to separate your good deeds from your bad deeds by attributing them to different 'sources'—perhaps circumstances or moods. But Ja'far's argument shows you are one single accountable soul before Allah. Will you today stop excusing your sins as coming from a 'different part of you' and instead own them completely as your own, seeking sincere tawbah?
?
When you apologize, is it merely an 'expression of pain' like beating a donkey, or is it a genuine acknowledgment of wrongdoing? Ja'far exposed the dualist leader's cheap excuse. What cheap excuses do you offer to Allah or to others, and when will you offer a true apology that brings you closer to righteousness?
?
The dualist converted when he saw the inconsistency in his worldview. What inconsistency in your own life—a gap between what you believe and how you act—are you ignoring? Will you let this lesson force you to confront it today, or will you remain comfortable in contradiction?

Why the Mu'tazilite Approach to Evil is Flawed
Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazilite method of debating dualists about evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the Mu'tazilite method of debating dualists about evil to be flawed and how it unintentionally reinforces dualism.

Core Idea
Denying real evil while debating dualists leads to self-defeating logic.

Maturidi examines the Mu'tazilite position that evil is merely metaphorical. He argues that when debating dualists, conceding that some things appear evil (even metaphorically) and then trying to affirm one Creator leads to a logical trap: it implies two creators. This passage exposes the weakness of the Mu'tazilite approach and shows how it inadvertently supports the dualist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Maturidi's dense style come through.
The passage contains a critique followed by an argument sequence.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim and Maturidi's critique
Note the Mu'tazilite stance that evil is not real but metaphorical. Then find Maturidi's charge: that this method is 'wrong' and leads to dualism.
Maturidi uses the term 'خطأ' (wrong) to open his critique.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the reasoning: If the Mu'tazilites concede that some things are called evil (even metaphorically), and then argue for one Creator, they end up with the dualist conclusion that the creator of evil is separate from the creator of good.
The key phrase is 'يلزمه التوحيد بالتثنية' – it necessitates oneness by dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة على قول المعتزلة خطأ؛ إذ من مذهبهم أن ليس في خلق الله شر وإنما سمي شرا بالمجاز، فإنما طريق مناظرتهم الثنوية في إزالة ما ظنوه شرا أن يكون شرا، فأما أن يسلموا الثنوية ويلزمهم القول بالخالق الواحد من الوجه الذي يوجد من غير الله تعالى الوجهين جميعا ويجعلونه على الصانع فيجد القول بنفي ذلك، فهو محال فاسد؛ لما فيه تثبيت معرفته وتوحيده يخلق الشر والخير ثم ينفي أحدهما الحقيقة، رجعت إلى قول الثنوية بأن الذي منه خلق الشر في الحقيقة غير الذي منه خلق الخير، فيلزمه التوحيد بالتثنية، ووجه قولهم في هذا أنهم أنكروا خلق أفعال العباد بما فيها السيئات والمعاصي والشرور والشرور، فعورضوا بخلق الشرور من الجواهر، وأنه لم يسم به شريرا ولا مسيئا، ولا إفساد الأشياء مفسدا، فكذلك في خلق أفعال الشر والفساد لا يسمى به، فكان من جوابهم أن الجواهر سميت شرا على المجاز لا على الحقيقة، وهي في الحقيقة ليست بالشر.
The issue according to the Mu'tazilites is wrong; because from their doctrine is that there is no evil in God's creation, rather it is called evil metaphorically. So the way to debate the dualists is to remove what they think is evil from being evil. But as for conceding to the dualists and imposing upon them the statement of the one Creator from the aspect that exists from other than God Almighty both aspects, and making it upon the Maker, and finding the statement negating that, it is impossible and corrupt; because it establishes (the dualists') knowledge and His oneness, He creates evil and good then negates one of them in reality, (this) returns to the statement of the dualists that He from whom the creation of evil truly is is other than He from whom the creation of good is, so it necessitates oneness by dualism. And the aspect of their statement in this is that they denied the creation of human actions including sins, disobediences, and evils, so they were countered by the creation of evils from substances, and that He is not called evil or wrongdoer for that, nor is the corruption of things called a corruptor, so likewise in the creation of acts of evil and corruption He is not called by it. So from their answer was that the substances are called evil metaphorically, not in reality, and they are in reality not evil.
Source pages p. 768

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazilite position on evil. The Mu'tazilites deny that God creates real evil; they say evil is merely metaphorical. Maturidi argues that this method of debating dualists is flawed. If the Mu'tazilites concede that some things appear evil (even if metaphorically), they implicitly accept the dualist premise that there is real evil. Then when they argue for one Creator, they end up with a contradiction: God creates both good and evil but then denies one in reality, which leads back to dualism. The Mu'tazilites counter that God creates harmful substances but is not called evil for that, and similarly for actions. The dualists respond that substances are called evil metaphorically, not in reality. Maturidi implies that this debate is circular and the Mu'tazilite approach is ineffective.

Key Terms
معتزلة
شر بالمجاز
ثنوية
خلق أفعال العباد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that God creates real evil solves the problem of evil and refutes dualism.
Correction: Maturidi shows that denying real evil while debating dualists actually reinforces dualism, because it concedes that some things are evil (even metaphorically) and then leads to the conclusion that the creator of evil must be separate from the creator of good.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the Mu'tazilite approach to evil?
A) It is correct because evil is metaphorical
B) It is wrong because it concedes to dualism
C) It is the only valid approach
D) It is incomplete but acceptable

Key Takeaway
Maturidi warns that the Mu'tazilite denial of real evil, when debating dualists, inadvertently reinforces the dualist worldview.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever explained away evil in your own life as merely metaphorical or insignificant, refusing to confront the real pain and the question it raises about God? What would it mean for you to honestly admit that evil is real while still trusting God's goodness?
?
When you face suffering, do you tend to shift blame to a source other than God, even if just in your heart? How does Maturidi's warning about inadvertently affirming dualism challenge you to rethink your response?
?
Are you willing to accept that God creates both good and evil, even when it is uncomfortable, or do you, like the Mu'tazilites, try to protect God's goodness by denying reality? What is one step you can take today to submit to God's sovereignty over all things, including suffering?

Refuting Dualists by Their Own Principles
Maturidi refutes dualists by their own claim that speech is by light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist doctrine by using the dualists' own claim that all speech proceeds from the substance of light.

Core Idea
Dualists' own actions disprove their theory: light produces both truth and falsehood.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise logical refutation of dualism. Instead of external arguments, he uses the dualists' own agreed principle—that they speak and act by the substance of light—to show the inconsistency of their belief. This passage demonstrates a powerful method of internal critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi begins with the dualists' agreement.
2
Identify the dualists' central claim
Find the claim that all speech and movement come from the substance of light. Understand that this is their own premise.
3
Follow the logical derivation
Trace how Maturidi derives contradictions: light yields both truth and falsehood, and dualists prefer light over darkness. See how this invalidates their core teaching.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض على الثنوية على اختلافهم اتفقوا في جميع ما ينطقون به أنهم بجوهر النور ينطقون و به يتقلبون، فصار كل الاختلاف به إن صدقوا، وإن كذبوا فصار كل الكذب به، وإن صدق بعضهم وكذب بعض، فثبت ممن هو من جوهر الظلمة تفضيل النور حتى اختار الانتساب إليه دون الظلمة، وتفضيل ذي الفضل خير في شهادة العقول، يلزم بطلان القول بأصل هو شر لا يجئ منه غيره، وخير لا يجئ منه غيره، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then what refutes the dualists, despite their differences, is that they all agree in all that they utter that they speak by the substance of light and by it they move about. So all difference (truth/falsehood) would be by it: if they are truthful, then all truth is by it; and if they are lying, then all falsehood is by it; and if some are truthful and some lying, then it is established that one who is from the substance of darkness prefers the light such that he chooses affiliation to it rather than darkness. And preferring a possessor of virtue is good in the testimony of intellects. This necessitates the invalidity of the statement that there is a principle that is only evil from which nothing but evil comes, and a principle that is only good from which nothing but good comes. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage is a logical refutation of dualism. Maturidi points out that all dualists, regardless of their sect, agree that they speak and act by the substance of light (جوهر النور). Therefore, everything they say—whether truth or falsehood—proceeds from light. If they speak truth, then light yields truth; if they lie, then light yields falsehood. Since we observe that some dualists lie, it follows that light is the source of falsehood as well, contradicting the claim that light is purely good. Furthermore, if some dualists are truthful and some liars, then it shows that someone originating from darkness (جوهر الظلمة) prefers light and chooses to affiliate with it. This preference for what is better is rationally good. Thus, the dualist assertion that there are two independent principles—one only good and one only evil—is invalid, because light produces both good and evil outcomes.

Key Terms
جوهر النور
جوهر الظلمة
تفضيل
شهادة العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Refutation of dualism requires lengthy proofs from revelation or philosophy.
Correction: Imam Maturidi shows a simple logical observation from their own premise suffices.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Maturidi draw from the dualists' claim that they speak by light?
A) They must be truthful
B) If they lie, light produces falsehood
C) Darkness is the source of speech
D) They cannot communicate

Key Takeaway
Dualists' own actions and statements prove that their light principle is not exclusively good, thus invalidating their core doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you blame your environment, upbringing, or 'inner darkness' for your sins, forgetting that your very act of choosing shows you know good from evil?
?
If you claim to believe in one Creator, why do you sometimes act as if evil has an independent power over you, excusing your wrong choices as inevitable?
?
In what specific area of your life are you living as though there are two origins for your actions—one good and one evil—while your own conscience testifies that you are responsible for preferring truth?

Refutation of the Dualists
Maturidi refutes dualists by showing their reliance on light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the dualist belief in two eternal principles by exposing an internal contradiction in their own practice.

Core Idea
Dualists themselves prefer light, proving its superiority and the falsehood of two equal origins.

Imam Maturidi engages with the dualist sects who claimed two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). He points out that despite their theoretical equality, in practice all dualists rely on light and even those from darkness prefer light. This logical inconsistency undermines their entire position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist claim
The dualists believe in two eternal, independent principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Grasp this foundational belief before considering Maturidi's critique.
2
Examine Maturidi's argument
Notice how Maturidi observes that all dualists, regardless of their differences, use 'the substance of light' to speak and act. Even those supposedly from darkness choose to affiliate with light.
This shows an inherent preference for light.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since preferring what is virtuous is rationally good, the preference for light over darkness indicates light's superiority. Therefore, the claim of two equal origins is invalid.
This refutes the dualist position from within their own practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي ينقض على الثنوية على اختلافهم اتفقوا في جميع ما ينطقون به أنهم بجوهر النور ينطقون و به يتقلبون ، فصار كل الاختلاف به إن صدقوا ، وإن كذبوا فصار كل الكذب به ، وإن صدق بعضهم وكذب بعض ، فثبت ممن هو من جوهر الظلمة تفضيل النور حتى اختار الانتساب إليه دون الظلمة ، وتفضيل ذي الفضل خير فى شهادة العقول ، يلزم بطلان القول بأصل هو شر لا يجئ منه غيره ، وخير لا يجئ منه غيره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then that which refutes the dualists, despite their differences, is that they all agree in everything they say that they speak by the substance of light and are transformed by it. So all difference becomes by it: if they speak truth, and if they lie, then all lying becomes by it, and if some speak truth and some lie, then it is established that whoever is of the substance of darkness prefers light, to the extent of choosing affiliation to it rather than darkness. And preferring the possessor of virtue is good in the testimony of intellects. This necessitates the invalidity of the statement that there is an origin that is evil from which nothing but evil comes, and a good from which nothing but good comes, and there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 769

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist sects (e.g., Manichaeans) who believe in two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Maturidi points out an internal contradiction: despite their differences, all dualists use the substance of light to speak and act; their very speech and actions depend on light. Moreover, even those supposedly from the substance of darkness prefer light and affiliate themselves with it. Since preferring what is better is rationally superior, this shows that light is superior to darkness. Therefore, the dualist claim that there are two equal origins—one purely good and one purely evil—is false because intellect would not prefer evil over good. Thus, the doctrine of two independent origins is invalid.

Key Terms
ثنوية
نور
ظلمة
تفضيل
أصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dualists consistently hold to equality of light and darkness, with no preference for either.
Correction: Maturidi shows that even those from the substance of darkness prefer light, choosing affiliation with light over darkness, which contradicts the supposed equality.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument Maturidi uses against the dualists?
A) They cannot prove the existence of darkness
B) Their own speech and actions depend on light, and even those of darkness prefer light
C) They deny free will
D) They do not believe in God

Key Takeaway
The dualist belief in two equal eternal principles is self-refuting because even those from darkness prefer light, demonstrating light's superiority and the invalidity of dual origins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in one God, but where do you actually turn when you need comfort, guidance, or relief? Do you rely on the ‘light’ of God or the ‘darkness’ of your own efforts? What would Maturidi’s argument reveal about your hidden dualism?
?
Dualists from darkness still preferred light. Is there any area in your life where you know the good (light) but habitually choose the bad (darkness)? How does this lesson expose the contradiction between your profession of faith and your actions?
?
If the intellect testifies that preferring what is good is virtuous, why do you sometimes find yourself making excuses for sin? What would it take for you to let that truth shatter your rationalizations and compel you to live consistently with tawheed?

Marcionite Cosmology: Three Principles and Mediator
Maturidi reports Marcionite and related dualist cosmologies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Marcionite doctrine of three principles (light, darkness, mediator) and their hierarchical arrangement, as described by Maturidi.

Core Idea
The Marcionites placed man as a mediator between light and darkness, neither wholly good nor evil.

Before we examine Maturidi's refutation, we must first understand what the Marcionites actually believed. They proposed a threefold cosmic structure of light above, darkness below, and a mediating human being in between. This lesson presents their cosmology as Maturidi reports it, including comparisons to other dualist sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the Marcionite doctrine
Read the Arabic passage describing the three principles: light, darkness, and the mediator (man). Notice that the mediator is neither light nor darkness.
Pay attention to the alignment analogy: like sun and shadow.
2
Identify the three principles and their arrangement
The Marcionites claimed light is above, darkness below, and a mediator between them. The mediator aligns with light above and darkness below.
The mediator is the sentient, perceiving human, who is life in a body.
3
Compare with Sabians and Manichaeans
Maturidi notes that Sabian and Manichaean views are similar, with only a small difference. Read the description of Manichaean cosmology.
Manichaeans believed light and darkness meet from opposite directions.
4
Reflect on the purpose of the mediator
Ask yourself: Why did the Marcionites need a mediator? How does it relate to the problem of good and evil?
This sets up Maturidi's refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً : أقاويل المرقيونية وبيان فسادها . والمرقيونية قالوا بعلو النور وسقول الظلمة وبمتوسط بينهما ليس بنور ولا ظلمة وهو الإنسان الحساس الدراك ، والإنسان عندهم حياة في البدن ، وأن هذه الثلاثة كانت متفرقة فامتزجت ، وأن كل جنس منها يحاذى الذي يليه كمحاذاة الشمس الظل نحو أعلى ، المتوسط يحاذى النور وأسفله الظلمة ، والجوهران عند الأولين كذلك في التحاذي . وقول الصابئين مثل قول المنانية إلا أن بينها - كما زعم ابن شبيب فرق قليل لا يحده . والمنانية زعمت أن النور يلقى الظلمة من الشمال ذاهبا فى مهب الجنوب ، والظلمة تلقاه فى مهب الجنوب ذاهبة فى مهب الشمال ، وكانا متلاقيين على دخول بعض الظلمة فيه ، ولا يتناهيان من سائر الجهات . فكلم هؤلاء بمثل الذي تكلم الثنوية . ثم لا يخلو الواسط من أن يكون تدبير كان منهما أمر العالم أو على الاجتماع حدث منه ، فان كان بالتدبير بطل الامتزاج ، وأني يقع وهو بين النور والظلمة ، والظلمة من شأنها التسفل ومن شأن النور العلو وبينهما فاصل يمنع إلا أن يكون بالتدبير جمع بينهما وامتزاج هو بهما فكان أصل كل شر ؛ إذ كان من الامتزاج، ولولا أنه مزج بينها ما وجد أحدهما سبيلا إلى الآخر فيصير الأمر إلى أن مدير الخير والشر واحد . وإن كانا هما غلبا بالطبع وقهرا الواسط حتى امتزجا فإذا لم ينفعه حسه ودركه ؛ إذ صار تحت قهر ذى الطبع ، فكونه واسطا لا معنى له ، أو حصل الأمر على النور والظلمة . ثم قالوا : جعلوا الواسط متناهيا والآخرين غير متناهيين ، والمتناهي تحت غير المتناهى ؛ لأنه كالمقصر عن تمام ما ليس بمتناه كالقصير من الطويل
Third: The sayings of the Marcionites and the exposition of their corruption. The Marcionites said that the light is above and the darkness is below, and between them there is a mediator that is neither light nor darkness, and it is the sentient, perceiving human. And man according to them is life in the body. And that these three were separate and then mixed. And that each kind of them aligns with the one next to it, like the alignment of the sun and the shadow upward; the mediator aligns with the light and its lower part with darkness, and the two substances according to the earlier ones are likewise in alignment. And the saying of the Sabians is like the saying of the Manichaeans, except that between them—as Ibn Shabib claimed—there is a small difference that he does not limit. And the Manichaeans claimed that light meets darkness from the north going toward the south wind, and darkness meets it in the south wind going toward the north wind, and they were meeting one another upon the entry of some darkness into it, and they do not extend from the other directions. So these spoke like what the dualists spoke. Then the mediator is not free from being either by design from them the affair of the world occurred, or by accidental mixing it happened. If it was by design, then mixing is invalid, and how could it occur while it is between light and darkness? Darkness is disposed to descend and light to ascend, and between them is a separator that prevents, unless by design He brought them together and mixing occurred by them, and then it would be the origin of every evil, since it came from mixing. And if it were not for mixing between them, neither would have found a way to the other, so the matter would lead to the manager of good and evil being one. And if both overcame by nature and compelled the mediator until they mixed, then his sense and perception do not benefit him since he became under the compulsion of the one with nature, so his being a mediator has no meaning; or the matter would revert to light and darkness. Then they said: They made the mediator finite and the others infinite, and the finite is under the infinite, because it is like falling short of the completeness of what is not finite, like the short compared to the tall.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Marcionite sect (a Gnostic Christian group). Maturidi first describes their doctrines: a threefold principle of light (good), darkness (evil), and a mediator (man) that is neither, with man being life in the body. They claim these were separate and then mixed, aligning hierarchically. He compares them to the Sabians and Manichaeans. The refutation proceeds in two steps: (1) The mediator must either result from design or accidental mixing. If by design, mixing is impossible because light and darkness have opposite natural tendencies (ascending vs descending) and a separator would prevent mixing—unless God Himself mixed them, which would make Him the origin of evil. If by accident, then the mediator's perception is useless under compulsion. (2) The mediator is finite while light and darkness are infinite, so the finite cannot effectively mediate between two infinites, just as a short person cannot match a tall one. Thus the Marcionite position is incoherent.

Key Terms
مرقيونية
نور
ظلمة
واسط
امتزاج
متناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mediator is a mixture of light and darkness.
Correction: The Marcionites held that the mediator is a third substance, neither light nor darkness, not a mixture.

Checkpoint
According to the Marcionites as described by Maturidi, what is the mediator (al-wasit)?
A) A mixture of light and darkness
B) Human beings, sentient and perceiving
C) A purely spiritual being
D) Darkness itself

Key Takeaway
The Marcionites proposed a three-tier cosmos with man as a mediator between light and darkness, a view shared with other dualist sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You read that the Marcionites placed man as a mediator between light and darkness, neither wholly good nor evil. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if you are a neutral observer, caught between right and wrong, rather than taking a decisive stand for the truth? What is one area where you have been hesitating to align fully with the light?
?
The Marcionites claimed the human is a 'mediator'—something in between. But the Quran teaches that humans are created with a pure nature. Have you allowed yourself to believe that you are inherently a mix of good and bad, excusing your sins by saying 'that's just human nature'? How does this lesson expose that as a flawed theology?
?
This passage describes a cosmology where light and darkness are separate then mixed. In your own heart, do you find a mixture of faith and hypocrisy? What specific step will you take today to separate yourself from the darkness and draw nearer to the light, as taught in the Quran and Sunnah?

Maturidi's Refutation: The Design vs. Accident Dilemma
Maturidi poses a dilemma to show the impossibility of the Marcionite mediator concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi’s argument against the Marcionite mediator using the dilemma of whether the mixing was by design or by accident.

Core Idea
If the mediator resulted from design, mixing is impossible; if by accident, the mediator’s consciousness is meaningless.

Having described the Marcionite doctrine, Maturidi now attacks its internal coherence. He presents a dilemma: the mixing of the three principles must have been either by deliberate design or by accidental encounter. Both options lead to absurdities, undermining the Marcionite system.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma passage
Read Maturidi's argument starting from 'Then the mediator is not free...' until the conclusion about a single manager.
Notice the two possibilities: design (tadbir) or accidental mixing (ijtima').
2
Analyze the design branch
If the mixing was by design, then the natural tendencies of light (to ascend) and darkness (to descend) would prevent mixing. A separator (fasil) would block them unless God Himself caused the mixing, which would make Him the origin of evil.
This leads to the conclusion that the manager of good and evil would be one.
3
Analyze the accident branch
If the mixing was accidental (by nature and compulsion), then the mediator's perception and consciousness are useless because he is overpowered by nature.
Thus his role as mediator becomes meaningless.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both branches lead to absurdities, showing the Marcionite position is incoherent.
This dialectic is typical of kalam refutations.

Maturidi's Refutation: The Finitude of the Mediator
Maturidi uses a finitude analogy to refute the Marcionite mediator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the finite mediator cannot effectively mediate between two infinite principles.

Core Idea
A finite being cannot bridge two infinite forces, just as a short person cannot match a tall one.

Maturidi presents a second, standalone argument against the Marcionite mediator: its finiteness. The Marcionites claimed the mediator is finite while light and darkness are infinite. This asymmetry makes the mediator incapable of its intended role, as the finite falls short of the infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the final argument
Read the short passage where Maturidi points out that the mediator is finite while the two principles are infinite.
Look for the analogy of the short and tall person.
2
Understand the analogy
The finite is under the infinite because it falls short of completeness, just as a short person is less than a tall one.
This is a clear, intuitive analogy.
3
Reflect on the consequence
If the mediator is finite, it cannot truly mediate between two infinites. Its role is meaningless.
This reinforces the earlier refutation.

The Marcionite Doctrine of Three Principles
Maturidi reports the Marcionite belief in three principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Marcionite claim of three principles (light, darkness, middle being) and how Maturidi situates it among dualist sects.

Core Idea
The Marcionites posited a middle being—the perceptive human—between light and darkness.

Maturidi reports an intriguing sectarian cosmology: the Marcionites believed in three eternal principles. Beyond the familiar dualism of light and darkness, they added a third entity: the perceptive human being. This lesson unpacks that doctrine and its place in Maturidi's doxography.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the Marcionite claim. Notice the terms used for the three principles.
The Arabic uses 'النور', 'الظلمة', and 'الواسط' for the principles.
2
Identify the three principles
List the three principles as stated: light (النور), darkness (الظلمة), and the middle being (الواسط). What is the middle being defined as?
The middle being is the perceptive human (الإنسان الحساس الدراك).
3
Compare with other sects
Maturidi briefly compares the Marcionites to the Sabians and Manichaeans. Note the similarities and the small difference mentioned.
The difference is small and undefined according to Ibn Shabib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرقيونية قالوا بعلو النور وسفول الظلمة وبمتوسط بينهما ليس بنور ولا ظلمة وهو الإنسان الحساس الدراك، والإنسان عندهم حياة في البدن، وأن هذه الثلاثة كانت متفرقة فامتزجت، وأن كل جنس منها يحاذى الذي يليه كمحاذاة الشمس الظل نحو أعلى، المتوسط يحاذى النور وأسفله الظلمة، والجوهران عند الأولين كذلك في التحاذي. وقول الصابئين مثل قول المنانية إلا أن بينها كما زعم ابن شبيب فرق قليل لا يحده. والمنانية زعمت أن النور يلقى الظلمة من الشمال ذاهبا في مهب الجنوب، والظلمة تلقاه في مهب الجنوب ذاهبة في مهب الشمال، وكانا متلاقيين على دخول بعض الظلمة فيه، ولا يتناهيان من سائر الجهات. فتكلم هؤلاء بمثل الذي تكلم الثنوية.
The Marcionites said: light is high and darkness is low, and there is a middle between them that is neither light nor darkness, and it is the perceptive human being. And the human being according to them is life in the body. And that these three were separate then mixed. And that each kind of them aligns with what follows it, like the alignment of the sun with the shadow upward; the middle aligns with light and its lower part with darkness. And the two substances according to the first ones are likewise in alignment. And the saying of the Sabians is like the saying of the Manichaeans, except that between them, as Ibn Shabib claimed, is a small difference that does not define it. And the Manichaeans claimed that light meets darkness from the north going into the direction of the south, and darkness meets it in the direction of the south going into the direction of the north, and they were meeting each other with some of the darkness entering into it, and they are not limited from other directions. So these spoke like what the dualists spoke.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage reports the Marcionite doctrine. Maturidi first states their claim that there are three principles: elevated light, low darkness, and a middle being that is neither light nor darkness. This middle being is the perceptive human. The human is defined as life in the body. The three were originally separate but then mixed. Each level aligns with the next: the middle aligns with light above and darkness below. Maturidi then compares this to the Sabians and Manichaeans, noting a small difference. He describes the Manichaean view of light and darkness meeting from opposite directions. He concludes that the Marcionites essentially follow the dualists.

Key Terms
النور
الظلمة
الواسط
الإنسان الحساس الدراك
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the middle being is a mixture of light and darkness.
Correction: The text explicitly states the middle is neither light nor darkness, but a perceptive human.

Checkpoint
According to the Marcionites, what is the middle being?
A) Light
B) Darkness
C) The perceptive human
D) A mixture of light and darkness

Key Takeaway
The Marcionites introduced a third principle—the perceptive human—between light and darkness, distinguishing them from simple dualists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you try to create a 'middle ground' between truth and falsehood, like the Marcionites did between light and darkness? What is one specific belief you are tempted to compromise on, and what will you do today to commit to the truth?
?
The Marcionites defined the human as 'life in the body'—a neutral substance. How does your understanding of the human soul from Islamic teachings differ from this materialistic view, and how should that change the way you live today?
?
Maturidi concluded that the Marcionites essentially spoke like dualists despite their third principle. Consider a belief you hold that you think is unique—could it be that it actually aligns with something you claim to reject? What step can you take to examine your beliefs more honestly?

Maturidi's Refutation of Marcionite Dualism: The Dilemma of the Middle
Maturidi refutes Marcionite dualism via a dilemma about the middle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical refutation of Marcionite dualism by analyzing the two possible origins of the middle (the human) and their implications.

Core Idea
Dualism collapses when forced to explain the middle: either it requires one governor or it negates itself.

This passage presents a tight logical dilemma against the Marcionites, who held that good and evil come from two separate principles. Maturidi asks a simple question: what about the human being, who experiences both? By examining the possible origins of this 'middle' being, he shows that either a single governor must be responsible, or the very concept of a middle becomes incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Marcionite claim
The Marcionites believed in two eternal principles: light (good) and darkness (evil). Maturidi focuses on the 'middle' – the human being – who experiences both good and evil.
This sets the stage for the dilemma.
2
Consider the first possibility: planning
If the middle is produced by the planning of light and darkness, then mixing would be unnatural and require a separate governor. Maturidi argues that this leads to the conclusion that the governor of good and evil is one.
Notice the phrase 'مدير الخير والشر واحد'.
3
Consider the second possibility: coercion
If the middle is overpowered by nature (light and darkness), then it loses its rational capacity and becomes meaningless. Thus, the concept of a middle being with perception collapses.
The middle's 'sense and perception' are negated.
4
Draw the conclusion
Both horns of the dilemma undermine Marcionite dualism. Either a single governor (Allah) is responsible, or the middle being is not a real independent entity.
Maturidi does not assert monotheism dogmatically; he shows it logically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا يخلو الواسط من أن يكون تدبير كان منهما أمر العالم أو على الاجتماع حدث منه. فإن كان بالتدبير بطل الامتزاج، وأنى يقع وهو بين النور والظلمة، والظلمة من شأنها التسفل ومن شأن النور العلو وبينهما فاصل يمنع إلا أن يكون بالتدبير جمع بينهما وامتزاج هو بهما فكان أصل كل شر؛ إذ كان من الامتزاج، ولولا أنه مزج بينها ما وجد أحدهما سبيلا إلى الآخر فيصير الأمر إلى أن مدير الخير والشر واحد. وإن كانا هما غلبا بالطبع وقهرا الواسط حتى امتزجا فإذا لم ينفعه حسه ودركه؛ إذ صار تحت قهر ذي الطبع، فكونه واسطا لا معنى له، أو حصل الأمر على النور والظلمة.
Then the middle is not free from either being a planning from the two of them for the affair of the world, or happening by coercion. If it is by planning, then the mixing is invalidated. And how can it occur while it is between light and darkness? Darkness's nature is to go low and light's nature is to go high, and between them is a barrier preventing it, unless by planning they were brought together and mixing occurred by them, and thus it became the origin of every evil, since it was from mixing. And were it not that He mixed between them, neither would have found a way to the other. So the matter leads to the conclusion that the governor of good and evil is one. And if it is the case that the two (light and darkness) overcame by nature and overpowered the middle until it mixed, then its sense and perception do not benefit it, since it fell under the coercion of those with nature. So its being a middle is meaningless; or the matter would be assigned to light and darkness.
Source pages p. 770

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Marcionite doctrine. Maturidi presents a dilemma: the existence of the middle (the human) is either by the planning of light and darkness or by their coercion. If by planning, then the mixing of opposites would not happen naturally, and the fact that mixing is the origin of evil implies that the governor of both good and evil is one. If by coercion, the middle loses its perceptive ability and thus its defining characteristic, making it meaningless. In either case, the Marcionite position collapses.

Key Terms
التدبير
الامتزاج
مدير الخير والشر
الطبع
القهر
الواسط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Marcionites believed that good and evil originate from two independent eternal principles (light and darkness).
Correction: Maturidi shows that this dualism leads to contradiction when explaining the human being, forcing either one governor or the denial of human rationality.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if the middle is produced by the planning of light and darkness?
A) The middle becomes divine
B) Mixing occurs naturally
C) The mixing is invalidated, leading to one governor
D) Light and darkness merge

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's dilemma forces the Marcionite to accept either a single governor of good and evil or to deny the existence of a rational middle being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose between right and wrong, do you ever feel like your decision is yours alone? How does Maturidi's argument that a single governor orchestrates all choices challenge your sense of autonomy? Will you pause before your next decision to acknowledge that your ability to choose is itself a gift from the One who governs all?
?
Is there an area of your life where you silently believe that evil comes from a source outside Allah's control—a person, a circumstance, or a 'force'? What would it mean for you to truly accept that the same Lord governs both good and evil? Can you name one grudge or fear you hold that dissolves when you internalize this truth?
?
You have a mind that can perceive and choose. Does that make you think you are independent of Allah's plan? Maturidi says that if you are not under His planning, your perception is meaningless. When you sin, are you acting as if your reason is self-sufficient? Commit today to recite 'There is no power or might except with Allah' before every action, to remind yourself that your very agency is dependent on Him.
